Rules of Differing in Sharecropping Contracts
End of Kitab al-Musaqah: procedural rulings for disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for determining the burden of proof in sharecropping disputes based on seed ownership and timing.

Core Idea
In sharecropping disputes, the seed owner's statement prevails after planting, but the landowner's evidence is preferred when both bring evidence.

Sharecropping contracts (musaqah and muzara'ah) often lead to disputes over validity or terms. The Hanafi school provides clear procedural rules to resolve them, primarily governed by who provided the seed and when the dispute arises. This lesson unpacks these rules as stated by the classical Hanafi jurists in the Book of Musaqah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the key factor
Recognize that the two parties are the landowner (rabb al-ard) and the worker (muzari'/amir). The critical factor in all disputes is who owns the seed (al-badhr).
Seed ownership is decisive in determining whose statement is accepted and whose evidence is preferred.
2
Distinguish timing: before vs after planting
Before planting, the one claiming the contract is corrupt (fasid) has the statement. After planting, the seed owner's statement is accepted. This protects the party who has invested resources.
The timing changes the presumption: before planting, invalidity is easier to claim; after planting, the contract is presumed valid unless the other party proves otherwise.
3
Apply the seed-ownership rule after planting
If the worker owns the seed, his statement is accepted after planting. If the landowner owns the seed, the worker's statement is accepted, but the landowner's evidence is required to override. This rule holds whether the dispute is before or after planting.
When the landowner owns the seed, the worker is given the statement because he is the one who labored, but the landowner can prove his claim with evidence.
4
Extend to the heirs after death
If the parties die, similar rules apply to their heirs. The seed owner's heirs have the stronger position: their statement is accepted for the shares, and if both bring evidence, the seed owner's evidence is preferred.
The principle of protecting the seed owner continues even after death.
5
Address post-harvest liability
If the worker dies after harvesting without accounting for his share, it is a trust (wadi'ah) and his estate is liable. If fruit matured and is missing, the worker guarantees the landowner's share if the fruit's emergence is known.
This protects the landowner's share when the worker fails to deliver after the crop is ready.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المساقاة
يتحالفان وبديء بيمين رب الأرض، وأما لو اختلفا في الجواز والفساد فهو على ثلاثة أوجه : إما إن اختلفا قبل الزراعة فالقول لمدعي الفساد وإن اختلفا بعد الزراعة فالقول لصاحب البذر. هذا إذا كان البذر من قبل العامل، فإن كان من قبل رب الأرض فاختلفا فالقول للعامل والبينة لرب الأرض، سواء اختلفا قبل الزرع أو بعده، وأما لو اختلفت ورثتهما بعد موتهما فإما أن يختلفا في مقدار الأنصباء والبذر من قبل العامل فالقول لورثة صاحب الأرض والبينة للآخر، وإن كان البذر من رب الأرض فالقول لورثة صاحب البذر والبيئة للآخر، وإن أقاما معاً بينة فبينة صاحب البذر ،أولى، وإن اختلفا في صاحب البذر كان القول قول ورثة المزارع والبينة للآخر، وإن اختلفا في البذر وفي شرط وأقاما بينة فالبينة بينة رب الأرض. ولو مات المزارع بعد الاستحصاد ولم يدر ما فعل بحصة المزارع فضمان حصة المزارع في ماله لأنه مات مجهلاً للوديعة، ولو مات العامل بعدما انتهت الثمرة فلم يوجد في النخل شيء إن علم خروج الثمرة ضمن حصة الآخر وإلا فلا كذا في المحيط. وتفاصيله تطلب منه اهـ والله تعالى أعلم بالصواب وإليه المرجع والمآب.
The Book of Musaqah: They take oaths and the oath of the landowner is begun. As for if they differ regarding validity and corruption, it is in three aspects: either they differ before planting, then the statement is of the one claiming corruption; if they differ after planting, then the statement is of the owner of the seed. This is when the seed is from the worker. If it is from the landowner and they differ, then the statement is of the worker and the evidence is for the landowner, whether they differ before or after planting. As for if their heirs differ after their deaths, then either they differ in the amount of shares and the seed is from the worker, then the statement is of the heirs of the landowner and evidence for the other; if the seed is from the landowner, then the statement is of the heirs of the seed owner and evidence for the other. If both bring evidence, the evidence of the seed owner is given preference. If they differ over who owns the seed, the statement is of the heirs of the sharecropper and evidence for the other. If they differ over the seed and a condition and bring evidence, the evidence is the evidence of the landowner. If the sharecropper dies after harvesting and it is not known what he did with his share, the guarantee of his share is in his wealth because he died unknown regarding the trust. If the worker dies after the fruit has matured and nothing is found in the palm trees, if the emergence of the fruit is known, he guarantees the share of the other; otherwise no. Thus in al-Muhit. Its details are sought from it. And Allah knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and abode.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage from the end of the Book of Musaqah (sharecropping contracts) presents rulings for disputes between the parties (landowner and worker) and their heirs. The passage is a series of rulings without proofs, typical of fiqh manuals. It first covers cases where the parties differ on whether the contract is valid or corrupt. Before planting, the one claiming corruption (invalidity) has the benefit of the doubt; after planting, the seed owner prevails. The seed ownership determines who has the stronger claim: if the seed is from the worker, his statement regarding the contract's validity is accepted later; if from the landowner, the worker's statement is accepted but the landowner must provide evidence. When both parties bring evidence, the seed owner's evidence is preferred. After death, similar rules apply to heirs, with the seed owner's evidence again taking precedence. The final paragraph deals with cases after maturity: if the worker dies without accounting for his share, it is a trust (wadi'ah) and his estate is liable; if the fruit is known to have emerged, he is liable for the landowner's share.

Key Terms
البذر (al-badhr): The seeds used for planting in a sharecropping contract; ownership of seed determines certain procedural rules in disputes.
المزارعة (al-muzara'ah): Sharecropping contract where landowner provides land and worker provides labor; here used generically for both muzara'ah and musaqah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when both parties bring evidence in a sharecropping dispute, whose evidence is given preference?
A) The landowner's evidence
B) The worker's evidence
C) The seed owner's evidence
D) Both are equal

Key Takeaway
In sharecropping disputes, the seed owner's statement or evidence is generally given greater weight, especially after planting, to protect the invested party.

Definition of Dhabh and Its Permissibility in the Hanafi School
Defines dhabh, cites evidences, distinguishes optional and emergency slaughter, refutes rational objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of dhabh (slaughter), distinguish optional and emergency slaughter, and explain why it is permissible by Shari'ah despite rational objections.

Core Idea
Slaughter (dhabh) is defined as cutting the jugular veins (awdaj) to permit the meat, and its permissibility is established by revelation, not reason.

This lesson defines the central act of slaughter (dhabh) in Islamic law, which is the means by which animals become halal for consumption. The Hanafi school, relying on the hadith 'Sever the jugular veins with whatever you wish,' specifies that the cut must include three of the four passages. We will also explore the distinction between optional and emergency slaughter, and address a rationalist objection refuted by Imam al-Sarakhsi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its definition
Read the text to extract the definition of dhabh and the specific parts (awdaj). Note that 'awdaj' is used generically to include windpipe, esophagus, and jugulars.
Hanafi: must cut three of four passages.
2
Locate the evidence for the ruling
Identify the hadith and Quranic proof cited (hadith 'أفر الأوداج' and Quran 5:3). Classify the hadith's epistemological weight.
The hadith is ahad (zanni) but sufficient for the ruling.
3
Distinguish between ikhtiyari and idtirari slaughter
Note that the primary method is cutting the neck between the collarbone and jawbones; the emergency method is any wound anywhere. Understand the principle that the emergency method is a substitute, used only when unable to perform the ideal.
The emergency method is less effective but valid due to necessity.
4
Examine the rational prohibition claim
Understand the objection raised by some Iraqi Hanafis: slaughter causes pain and is rationally prohibited. This is a claim that reason alone could deem it prohibited.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf that was later rejected.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response via Sarakhsi
Observe how al-Sarakhsi refutes the claim by pointing to the Prophet's practice: he slaughtered and ate meat before prophethood, and would not do something rationally prohibited like lying or injustice. This shows that permissibility is established by revelation and confirmed by the Prophet's action.
The correct position is that slaughter is mubah (permissible) and not rationally prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذبح قطع الأوداج لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام "أفر الأوداج بما شئت" والمراد الحلقوم والمريء والودجان، وإنما عبر بالأوداج تغليباً وبه يحل المذبوح لقوله تعالى {إلا ما ذكيتم} ولأن المحرم هو الدم المسفوح وبالذبح يقع التمييز بينه وبين اللحم فيطهر به إن كان غير مأكول ويقال ذكاء السن بالمد لنهاية الشباب، وذكاة النار بالقصر لتمام اشتعالها وهي اختيارية واضطرارية فالأول الجرح ما بين اللبة واللحيين، والثاني الجرح في أي موضع كان من البدن وهذا كالبدل عن الأول لأنه لا يصار إليه إلا عند العجز عن الأول، وإنما كان كذلك لأن الأول أبلغ في إخراج الدم من الثاني فلا يترك إلا بالعجز عنه، ويكتفي بالثاني للضرورة لأن التكليف بحسب الوسع . وذهب العراقيون من مشايخنا إلى أن الذبح محظور عقلاً لما فيه من إيلام الحيوان ولكن الشرع أحلة. قال شمس الأئمة السرخسي في المبسوط : وهذا عندي باطل لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام كان يتناول اللحم قبل البعثة ولا يظن به أنه كان يأكل ذبائح المشركين لذبحهم بأسماء الهتهم فعرفنا أنه كان يصطاد ويذبح بنفسه وما كان يفعل ما هو المحظور عقلاً كالكذب والظلم والسفه.
And slaughter is the cutting of the jugular veins due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Sever the jugular veins with whatever you wish.' And what is intended is the windpipe, the esophagus, and the two jugular veins. He only expressed it as 'jugular veins' by way of predominance. And by it the slaughtered animal becomes permissible due to the saying of the Exalted: 'Except what you slaughter.' And because what is prohibited is the spilled blood, and by slaughter the distinction is made between it and the meat, so it becomes purified thereby if it was not edible. And it is said 'zakaa' of the tooth with elongation for the end of youth, and 'zakaa' of the fire with shortening for the completion of its blaze. And it is optional and emergency. The first is the wound between the collarbone and the jawbones, and the second is the wound in any place of the body. And this is like a substitute for the first because it is only resorted to when unable to do the first. And it was like that because the first is more effective in extracting blood than the second, so it is not abandoned except in inability. And the second suffices due to necessity because obligation is according to capacity. And the Iraqis among our masters held that slaughter is rationally prohibited due to the pain it causes to the animal, but the Law permitted it. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said in al-Mabsut: This is false in my view because he, peace be upon him, used to eat meat before his mission, and it is not thought that he used to eat the slaughtered animals of the polytheists due to their slaughtering in the name of their gods. So we know that he used to hunt and slaughter himself, and he would not do what is rationally prohibited like lying, injustice, and folly.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion on the definition of dhabh (slaughter) and its permissibility. The author, Ibn Nujaym, begins by stating the definition: dhabh is the cutting of the awdaj (jugular veins). He supports this with the hadith 'Sever the jugular veins with whatever you wish,' clarifying that awdaj here is used generically to include the windpipe (halqum), esophagus (mari'), and the two jugular veins (wadajan). The purpose of slaughter is to permit the meat, as the Quran says 'except what you slaughter' (5:3), and because the prohibited is the spilled blood. Through slaughter, the blood is separated from the meat, and even the meat of non-edible animals becomes pure (though not for eating). He then distinguishes between two types of dhabh: ikhtiyari (optional, the prescribed cut in the neck) and idtirari (emergency, a wound anywhere on the body when unable to do the neck cut). The latter is a substitute for the former, as it is less effective but suffices in necessity. The author then refutes a rationalistic objection from some Iraqi Hanafis who claimed that slaughter is rationally prohibited because it causes pain to animals. Sarakhsi rejects this, arguing that the Prophet himself slaughtered and ate meat before his prophethood, and he would not do something rationally prohibited like lying or injustice. Thus, the permissibility of slaughter is based on revelation, not reason, though reason does not condemn it.

Key Terms
الذبح (al-dhabh): Cutting the jugular veins (awdaj) to cause death and allow blood drainage.
الأوداج (al-awdaj): The jugular veins, but here used inclusively for windpipe, esophagus, and two jugulars.
ذكاة اختيارية (dhabh ikhtiyari): The primary, prescribed method of slaughter by cutting the neck in the designated area.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the definition of dhabh (slaughter)?
A) Cutting any part of the animal's body
B) Cutting the jugular veins (awdaj) including windpipe, esophagus, and jugulars
C) Killing the animal by any means
D) Stunning the animal before cutting

Key Takeaway
Dhabh is the cutting of the jugular veins (awdaj) to permit meat, and its permissibility is established by revelation, not reason, as demonstrated by the Prophet's own practice.

Permissibility of Meat from Muslims and People of the Book
States ruling with proof from Quran and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which meat slaughtered by a Muslim or Kitabi is halal and identify the Quranic proof.

Core Idea
Meat of a Muslim or Kitabi is halal unless they invoke other than Allah.

This passage clarifies the permissibility of meat slaughtered by a Muslim or a Person of the Book (Kitabi). It cites Quran 5:5 and establishes a crucial condition: the slaughterer must not invoke any name other than Allah during slaughter. The ruling applies equally to both Muslims and People of the Book, and includes children and the feeble-minded provided they can make intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The slaughtered meat (dhabiha) of a Muslim and a Kitabi. The ruling is halal (mubah).
2
Locate the primary dalil
The author cites Quran 5:5: 'The food of those who were given the Scripture is lawful for you.' Identify this as a direct textual proof.
The passage interprets 'food' as slaughtered meat.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the proof yield the ruling? The word 'food' is understood as their slaughtered meat, and 'lawful' indicates permissibility. The Hanafi school takes the general command as proof.
The dalala is considered zanni (probable) but sufficient for fiqh.
4
Note the condition (qayd)
The passage adds a condition from Quran 2:173: if the slaughterer invokes other than Allah (e.g., mentions Jesus or Ezra), the meat becomes haram. This applies to both Muslim and Kitabi.
The condition is that the name of Allah must be invoked, not another.
5
Understand the practical distinction regarding witnessing
Al-’Inaya distinguishes: if the Kitabi brings already slaughtered meat, we eat it assuming proper tasmiya. If we witness the slaughter, we must verify that no other name is invoked.
This shows the principle of default permissibility (istishab) versus direct observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وحل ذبيحة مسلم وكتابي لقوله تعالى {وطعام الذين أوتوا الكتاب حل لكم} [المائدة : ٥] والمراد به ذبائحهم لأن مطلق الطعام غير المذكى يحل من أي كافر ولا يشترط أن يكون من أهل الكتاب ولا فرق في الكتابي بين أن يكون ذمياً أو حربياً، ويشترط أن لا يذكر فيه غير الله تعالى حتى لو ذكر الكتابي المسيح أو عزيراً لا يحل لقوله تعالى {وما أهل به لغير الله} [البقرة : ۱۷۳] وهو كالمسلم في ذلك فإنه لو أهل به لغير الله لا يحل. قال في العناية : الكتابي إذا أتى بالذبيحة مذبوحة أكلنا فلو ذبح بالحضور فلا بد من الشرط وهو أن لا يذكر عليها غير اسم الله . ولا فرق في الذابح بين أن يكون صبياً أو مجنوناً. قال في النهاية : المراد بالمجنون المعتوه لأن المجنون لا قصد له ولا بد من التسمية وهي القصد أن يعقلها.
He (the author) said: And the slaughtered meat of a Muslim and a Kitabi is permissible due to the saying of the Exalted: 'The food of those who were given the Scripture is lawful for you' (5:5). And what is meant by it is their slaughtered animals, because absolute non-slaughtered food is permissible from any disbeliever, and it is not required that they be from the People of the Book. And there is no difference in the Kitabi between being a dhimmi or a harbi. And it is required that he does not mention other than Allah upon it, such that if the Kitabi mentions the Messiah or Ezra, it is not permissible due to the saying of the Exalted: 'And that over which the name of other than Allah has been invoked' (2:173). And he is like the Muslim in that, for if he invokes other than Allah, it is not permissible. He said in al-’Inaya: If the Kitabi brings the slaughtered animal already slaughtered, we eat it. However if he slaughters in our presence, then the condition is necessary that he does not mention other than Allah upon it. And there is no difference in the slaughterer between being a child or an insane person. He said in al-Nihaya: What is meant by the insane is the feeble-minded because the insane has no intention, and the tasmiya is necessary, which is the intention that he comprehends it.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage addresses the permissibility of meat slaughtered by two categories: Muslims and People of the Book (Jews and Christians). The proof is Quran 5:5 'the food of those who were given the Scripture is lawful for you', where 'food' is interpreted as their slaughtered meat. The author clarifies that the ruling is specific to slaughter, not just any food. It applies to all People of the Book regardless of their political status (dhimmis or harbis). However, the condition is that they do not invoke anyone other than Allah during slaughter. If a Jew mentions the Messiah or a Christian mentions Ezra, the meat is not halal based on Quran 2:173 which prohibits meat over which Allah's name is not mentioned. The ruling is the same as for a Muslim: if they deliberately invoke another, the meat is unlawful. The commentary from al-’Inaya adds that if the Kitabi brings already slaughtered meat, we eat it assuming it was done properly, but if we witness the slaughter, we must ensure no other name is invoked. The passage also mentions that the slaughterer can be a child or an insane person (feeble-minded), as long as they are capable of comprehending and making the intention (tasmiya). This is because the requirement is that the act is done with awareness and with the name of Allah.

Key Terms
كتابي (kitabi): A Jew or Christian who possesses a revealed scripture.
مجنون (majnoon): In this context, the feeble-minded (ma'tuh) who has some intent, not a total madman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a Christian slaughters an animal while mentioning the name of Jesus (Isa), is the meat halal?
A) Yes, because he is a Kitabi
B) No, because he invoked other than Allah
C) Yes, because it is still Muslim slaughter
D) No, because Christians are not considered People of the Book

Key Takeaway
Meat slaughtered by a Muslim or Kitabi is halal unless they invoke other than Allah during slaughter.

Validity of Slaughter by Child, Woman, Mute, and Uncircumcised
Ruling that slaughter by certain categories is valid under conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which slaughter by a child, woman, mute, or uncircumcised person is valid in the Hanafi school and apply the ruling to identify when such meat is halal.

Core Idea
A valid slaughterer need not be an adult male; anyone capable of intention and proper method suffices.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author clarifies that the meat of an animal slaughtered by a child, a woman, a mute, or an uncircumcised person is permissible (halal) provided certain conditions are met. The key is that the slaughterer must have the knowledge and ability to perform the slaughter correctly and to intend the tasmiya (mentioning Allah's name). This expands the common assumption that only adult males can slaughter, and highlights the Hanafi emphasis on capability and intention over gender or age.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
List the four types of people mentioned: child (sabi), woman (imra'ah), mute (akhras), and uncircumcised (aqlaf).
2
Determine the condition for validity
The text states that the child must 'comprehend the tasmiya and take control' – i.e., know the method of slaughter (cutting the jugular veins) and be able to execute it. The same applies to the woman and uncircumcised.
If the child is not discerning (ghayr mumayyiz), the meat is not halal.
3
Analyze the special case of the mute
The mute is unable to verbally pronounce the tasmiya. The Hanafi ruling excuses him: his intention (niyyah) substitutes for the verbal declaration, as the 'religion (millah) takes his place' – similar to the forgetful person who omits tasmiya. In fact, his case is stronger because his inability is permanent.
This is a concession (rukhsah) for the mute.
4
Apply the ruling to the uncircumcised
The text includes 'aqlaf' (uncircumcised). If he meets the conditions of knowledge and control, his slaughter is permissible, even if he has not performed circumcision.
Circumcision is not a condition for the validity of slaughter in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال. رحمه الله : (وصبي وامرأة وأخرس وأقلف) يعني تحل ذبيحة هؤلاء. والمراد بالصبي الذي يعقل التسمية ويضبط وإن لم يكن كذلك لا يحل لأن التسمية على الذبيحة شرط بالنص وذلك بالعقد وصحة العقد بالمعرفة والضبط هو أن يعلم شرائط الذبح من فري الأوداج والتسمية والمعتوه كالصبي إذا كان صابطاً والقلقة ولا الفراسة لا تحل بذلك فيحل والأخرس عاجز عن الذكر فيكون معذوراً وتقوم الملة مقامه كالناسي بل أولى لأنه ألزم.
He said: (And a child, a woman, a mute, and an uncircumcised) meaning the slaughtered meat of these is permissible. And what is meant by the child is one who comprehends the tasmiya and takes control. If he is not like that, it is not permissible because tasmiya upon the slaughtered animal is a condition by text, and that is by contract, and the validity of the contract is by knowledge and control. And control is that he knows the conditions of slaughter from cutting the jugular veins and the tasmiya. And the feeble-minded is like the child if he is in control. And the female and the uncircumcised? (unclear) and the perceptive? It does not become permissible by that, so it is permissible. And the mute is unable to mention, so he is excused, and the religion takes his place like the forgetful, nay rather more so because it is more binding.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling that slaughter by a child, a woman, a mute, or an uncircumcised person is valid and the meat is halal. The key condition is that the person must have the ability to comprehend and intend the tasmiya (mentioning Allah's name) and must know the proper method of slaughter (cutting the jugular veins). For a child, if he understands and can control the act, it is valid; otherwise not. The 'feeble-minded' (ma'tuh) is treated like a child if he can control himself. The text mentions 'al-qalqah' (perhaps a typo for 'al-mar'ah'? Actually it might be 'al-aghalf' - uncircumcised? The phrase is unclear but the ruling is that it is permissible). The mute is excused from the verbal tasmiya, and his intention substitutes for it, similar to the forgetful person. In fact, his case is even stronger because he is permanently unable to speak. The religion (millah) stands in for his inability, meaning the act is deemed to be performed with the proper intention.

Key Terms
صبي (sabi): A child who comprehends tasmiya and can control the slaughter.
أخرس (akhras): A mute person who cannot verbally mention Allah's name.
أقلف (aqlaf): An uncircumcised person (male).
ضبط (dabt): Control – both knowledge of the conditions and ability to perform the slaughter properly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of meat slaughtered by a child who understands the tasmiya and knows how to cut the jugular veins but forgets to say 'Bismillah'?
A) Halal, because he is capable and the tasmiya is intended.
B) Not halal, because the child forgetfulness invalidates the slaughter.
C) Halal, but only if an adult repeats the tasmiya.
D) Not halal, because children cannot slaughter.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the validity of slaughter depends on the slaughterer's knowledge and control, not on gender, age, or circumcision status, and the mute's intention substitutes for verbal tasmiya.

Prohibition of Meat from Majus, Idolater, and Apostate
States the invalidity of slaughter by Magians, idolaters, and apostates, with exceptions for converts between Kitabi religions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the permissibility of meat slaughtered by Magians, idolaters, and apostates, and explain the exceptions for converts between People of the Book.

Core Idea
Magians, idolaters, and apostates cannot slaughter halal meat — but a Kitabi who switches Kitabi religions remains valid.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies who can lawfully slaughter animals for Muslim consumption. While Muslims and People of the Book (Jews, Christians) are generally allowed, the Hanafi school excludes Magians (Zoroastrians), idolaters, and apostates. The text also addresses how conversion between religions affects the ruling, providing nuanced exceptions that are essential for proper halal certification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited categories
List the types of individuals whose slaughtered meat is not halal according to the passage: Magians, idolaters, apostates, those in ihram (for hunting), and those who intentionally omit tasmiya. Focus on the first three as they are elaborated.
The categories of Muhrim and tasmiya omission are mentioned but not detailed here.
2
Understand the hadith prohibition for Magians
The Prophet ﷺ said to treat Magians as People of the Book in customs, but not to marry their women nor eat their slaughtered animals. This hadith is the primary evidence. Analyze why: Magians lack a heavenly scripture and thus do not have valid monotheistic belief.
The Hanafi school uses this hadith to distinguish Magians from People of the Book.
3
Apply analogy to idolaters
Idolaters are likened to Magians because both are polytheists. By qiyas, the same prohibition applies. No separate proof is needed.
This shows how Hanafi fiqh extends rulings via illah (effective cause).
4
Explain the apostate rule and conversion exceptions
An apostate (one who leaves Islam) cannot slaughter at all, regardless of the religion he adopts. However, a Jew who becomes Christian, a Christian who becomes Jew, or a Magian who becomes Jew or Christian is considered a Kitabi and his slaughter becomes valid. The key is that he is now 'confirmed' in a Kitabi religion. The reverse (Jew becomes Magian) invalidates.
This exception applies only to converts from one Kitabi religion to another, or from Magian to Kitabi.
5
Note the ruling for offspring of mixed marriages
The child of a Kitabi and a Magian is considered a Kitabi, following the father or the higher status (likely the Kitabi parent). This has implications for who can slaughter.
This is an additional detail from the Sharh al-Tahawi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (لا مجوسي ووثني ومرتد ومحرم وتارك التسمية (عمداً يعني لا تحل ذبيحة هؤلاء؛ أما المجوسي فلقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «سنوا بهم سنة أهل الكتاب غير ناكحي نسائهم ولا آكلي ذبائحهم لأنه ليس له دين سماوي فانعدم التوحيد اعتقاداً ودعوى. والوثني كالمجوسي فيما ذكرنا لأنه مشرك مثله، وأما المرتد فلأنه لا يقر على ما انتقل إليه ولهذا لا يجوز نكاح بخلاف اليهودي إذا تنصر وبالعكس أو تنصر المجوسي أو تهود لأنه يقر على ما انتقل إليه عندنا فتؤكل ذبيحته ولو تمجس اليهودي لا تؤكل ذبيحته ولا فرق في المرتد بين أن يرتد إلى دين اليهودية أو النصرانية أو إلى غير ذلك ؛ كذا في شرح الطحاوي والمتولد بين الكتابي والمجوسي يعتبر كتابياً.
He said: (Not a Magian, idolater, apostate, Muhrim, and one who intentionally leaves the tasmiya) meaning the slaughtered meat of these is not permissible. As for the Magian, due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Treat them according to the custom of the People of the Book, but do not marry their women nor eat their slaughtered animals' because he has no heavenly religion, so there is an absence of monotheism in belief and claim. And the idolater is like the Magian in what we have mentioned because he is a polytheist like him. As for the apostate, because he is not confirmed in that to which he has transferred, and for this reason his marriage is not valid, contrary to the Jew if he becomes Christian, and vice versa, or the Magian becomes Christian or Jewish, because he is confirmed in that to which he has transferred according to us, so his slaughter is eaten. But if the Jew becomes a Magian, his slaughter is not eaten. And there is no difference in the apostate between apostatizing to Judaism, Christianity, or anything else. Thus in Sharh al-Tahawi. And the child born between a Kitabi and a Magian is considered a Kitabi.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage enumerates categories of people whose slaughter is not permissible for Muslims to eat. These are: Magians (Zoroastrians), idolaters (polytheists), apostates from Islam, those in ihram (Muhrim) for hunting, and those who intentionally omit the tasmiya (mentioning Allah's name). First, Magians: the Prophet forbade eating their slaughtered animals in a hadith that says treat them as People of the Book in certain aspects but not in marriage or eating slaughtered animals. The reason given is that they have no heavenly scripture, so their belief lacks monotheism. Idolaters are similar as polytheists. For an apostate: his slaughter is invalid because he is not confirmed in the religion he adopted; thus his marriage is also invalid. However, if a Jew becomes Christian or vice versa, or a Magian becomes Christian or Jew, his slaughter is permissible because he is considered confirmed in the religion he converted to (i.e., he is now a Kitabi). If a Jew becomes a Magian, his slaughter is not permissible because he left a Kitabi religion for a non-Kitabi one. The apostate ruling applies regardless of what religion he apostatizes to. Finally, the offspring of a Kitabi and a Magian assumes the status of the Kitabi (likely following the higher status).

Key Terms
مجوسي (majusi): A follower of Zoroastrianism (Magian).
مرتد (murtadd): A Muslim who leaves Islam for another religion or apostasy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is the meat of an animal slaughtered by a Muslim who apostatizes to Christianity halal?
A) Yes, because Christianity is a Kitabi religion
B) No, because his apostasy invalidates his slaughter
C) Yes, if he repents afterwards
D) No, only if he was originally a Christian

Key Takeaway
Meat is halal only if the slaughterer is a Muslim or a confirmed Kitabi; Magians, idolaters, and apostates are never valid.

Ruling on Slaughter by Muhrim and Intentional Omission of Tasmiya
Explains two rulings: Muhrim's game slaughter is invalid; intentional omission of tasmiya is haram (dispute with Shafi'i).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the permissibility of meat from a Muhrim's slaughter of game, and explain why intentionally omitting tasmiya renders meat haram, while distinguishing the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
A Muhrim's game slaughter is invalid; intentional omission of tasmiya makes meat haram, even if a judge rules otherwise.

This passage continues from a list of categories whose slaughtered animals are not lawful. It first addresses the Muhrim (one in ihram) and extends the prohibition to non-Muhrim hunting in the Haram. Then it turns to a key point of difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: whether deliberately omitting the tasmiya (Bismillah) during slaughter makes the meat impermissible. The Hanafi school, relying on Quran and Hadith, holds that it does, and considers this a matter of consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage discusses two rulings: (a) Muhrim's slaughter of game, and (b) intentional omission of tasmiya. Note that the first is extended by analogy to non-Muhrim and Kitabi in the Haram.
These are two distinct issues; the first is not disputed, the second is.
2
Analyze the Muhrim ruling
The Muhrim's action regarding game is not legislated (ghayr mashru'), so the meat is not permissible. This is a straightforward ban.
The prohibition is specific to game; domestic animals slaughtered by Muhrim are halal.
3
Examine the tasmiya dispute
The author cites Quran 6:121 and a hadith to support that meat without tasmiya is not permissible. The Shafi'i school disagrees. Note the restriction to intentional omission; forgetful omission is permissible.
The Shafi'i view is that tasmiya is recommended, not obligatory.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi argument
Abu Yusuf and the mashayikh assert that this is not a matter open to ijtihad because it contradicts consensus; thus even a judge's ruling to permit it is void.
This elevates the ruling from a mere opinion to a definitive position.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafi schools prohibit consumption and sale of such meat, while Shafi'i permits it.
This difference affects everyday transactions and judicial rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المحرم فالمراد به في حق الصيد لأن ذبيحته في حق الصيد لا تؤكل لأن فعله فيه غير مشروع، وكذا الحلال في حق صيد الحرم، وكذا الكتابي لو ذبح صيداً في الحرم لا يحل أكله . وأما تارك التسمية عمداً فلقوله تعالى {ولا تأكلوا مما لم يذكر اسم الله عليه} [الأنعام: ۱۲۱] ولقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام إذا أرسلت كلبك المعلم وذكرت اسم الله فكل الحديث. وقال الشافعي: تؤكل قيدنا بقولنا عمداً لأنه لو ترك التسمية ناسياً يحل أكلها وهو مذهب علي وابن عباس وقال أبو يوسف والمشايخ: إن متروك التسمية عمداً لا يسوغ فيه الاجتهاد حتى لو قضى القاضي بجواز بيعه لا ينفذ قضاؤه لكونه مخالفاً للإجماع.
And as for the Muhrim, what is intended by it is with respect to hunting, because his slaughtered animal with respect to hunting is not eaten, because his action in it is not legislated. Likewise, the non-Muhrim with respect to game in the Haram, and likewise the Kitabi if he slaughters game in the Haram, its consumption is not permissible. And as for the one who intentionally leaves the tasmiya, due to the saying of the Exalted: 'And do not eat of that over which the name of Allah has not been mentioned' (6:121) and due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'If you send your trained dog and mention the name of Allah, then eat.' (Hadith). And al-Shafi'i said: It is eaten. We restricted by saying 'intentionally' because if he leaves the tasmiya forgetfully, its consumption is permissible, which is the school of Ali and Ibn Abbas. And Abu Yusuf and the mashayikh said: Regarding the one who intentionally leaves the tasmiya, ijtihad is not permissible in it, such that if a judge ruled permissibility of its sale, his judgment would not be executed because it opposes consensus.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage addresses two specific categories from the previous list: the Muhrim (one in state of ihram) and the one who intentionally omits tasmiya. For the Muhrim, the prohibition is specifically regarding hunting game in the state of ihram. A Muhrim's slaughter of game is not valid because his action is not legislated. Similarly, a non-Muhrim may not hunt in the Haram (sanctuary of Mecca), and even a Kitabi may not slaughter game in the Haram. For the one who intentionally omits tasmiya, the proof is Quran 6:121 and the hadith about sending a trained dog while mentioning Allah's name. The author notes that al-Shafi'i holds that the meat is still permissible even if tasmiya is intentionally omitted. The Hanafi response is that we distinguish between intentional and forgetful omission. If forgotten, the meat is halal, as reported from Ali and Ibn Abbas. Abu Yusuf and the elder scholars state that the case of intentional omission is not open to ijtihad because it contradicts consensus; thus even a judge's ruling that it is permissible would be invalid.

Key Terms
محرم (muhrim): A person in the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah.
تارك التسمية عمداً (tarik al-tasmiya amdan): One who deliberately refrains from saying Bismillah during slaughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, if he is a Muslim
B) No, it is haram because intentional omission invalidates
C) Yes, if he is a Kitabi
D) No, only if he is forgetful

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, intentional omission of tasmiya renders meat haram and even a judge's ruling to the contrary is void; forgetful omission is excused.

Tasmiya for Multiple Animals in One Act
Rulings on tasmiya for multiple animals in one act vs. separate acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that one tasmiya suffices for multiple animals slaughtered in one continuous act and distinguish scenarios where separate tasmiya is required.

Core Idea
One tasmiya suffices if the slaughter act is unified; separate animals require separate tasmiya unless physically connected in the act.

When slaughtering multiple animals, the need for repeating tasmiya depends on whether the killing act is one or separate. The source passage distinguishes between shooting an arrow at a group of game and separately slaughtering two sheep. This lesson unpacks the key scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the unity of the slaughter act
Determine if the animals are killed by a single action (e.g., one arrow, one knife cut across stacked sheep) or by sequential separate actions.
The unity of the act is key: one tasmiya for one act, multiple acts need multiple tasmiya.
2
Apply to the arrow-at-game scenario
If one arrow hits multiple game simultaneously, one tasmiya during shooting suffices for all because the act is one.
The arrow is the tool, but in emergency (idtirari) slaughter, tasmiya is on the tool, yet one act covers all.
3
Apply to the stacked sheep scenario
If two sheep are laid one on top of the other and slaughtered with one knife cut, one tasmiya suffices because the cut is one act.
Even though there are two animals, the act of slaughter is physically one.
4
Apply to separate slaughter of two sheep
If the first sheep is slaughtered with tasmiya and then the second without a new tasmiya, only the first is halal. Each requires its own tasmiya because the acts are distinct.
This is the default: each separate slaughter act needs its own tasmiya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ذبح شاتين فسمى على الأولى دون الثانية تحل الأولى دون الثانية، ولو سهماً إلى صيود فأثخن الكل يكفيه تسمية واحدة وإن حصل بها ذكاة صيود كثيرة، فأما ذبح الشاة الثانية فلا بد له من تسمية ثانية حتى لو أضجع شاتين إحداهما على الأخرى وذبحهما بحديدة يحلان بتسمية واحدة ولو أضجع شاة ليذبحها ثم ألقى تلك السكين وأخذ سكيناً أخرى لا بأس به بخلاف ما لو أخذ سهماً . وسمى فوضع ذلك ورفع آخر ولم يسم يحل أكله لأن التسمية في الذكاة الاختيارية مشروعة على الذبح لا على آلته والذبيحة لم تتغير، الذكاة الاضطرارية التسمية على الآلة لا على الذبيحة والآلة قد تغيرت. وعن أبي يوسف: ولو أضجع شاة وسمى فأرسلها وأخذ غيرها وذبحها بتلك التسمية لم تجز، ولو رمى إلى صيد فأصاب آخر يحل لما بينا . سمى واشتغل بآخر إن كان قليلاً كما لو كلم إنساناً أو شرب ماء يحل وإن كان طويلاً فلا لأن إيقاع الذبح متصل بالتسمية بحيث لا يتخلل بينهما شيء ولا يمكن إلا بجرح فأقيم المجلس مقام الإتصال والعمل القليل لا يقطع المجلس فيكون مذبوحاً على التسمية والكثير يقطع فيفصل بينهما فيكون مذبوحاً بغير تسمية. ولو قال الله جاز نوى أو لم ينو لأنه صريح في التسمية، وظاهر حاله يدل على أنه أراد به التسمية على الذبيحة فيقع عنها ما لم يوجد منه الصرف عنها حتى لو أراد به التسمية على غيره كمن قال الله أكبر وأراد به إجابة الأذان لا افتتاح الصلاة ولم يصر شارعاً فيها. ولو سبح أو حمد الله أو كبر يريد به التسمية على الذبيحة تحل وإلا فلا لأن هذه الألفاظ كناية عن والكناية إنما تقوم مقام الصريح بالنية. ولو قال الله بغير هاء الله إن أراد به التسمية يحل وإلا فلا لأن العرب قد تحذف حرفاً ترخيماً؛ كذا في المحيط. وفي التتمة: رجل ذبح وفي بسم الله جاز نوى أو لم ينو.
And if he slaughtered two sheep and said tasmiya on the first but not the second, the first is permissible and the second is not. And if he shot an arrow at a group of game and it killed all of them, one tasmiya is sufficient for all, even if it made many games lawful. As for slaughtering the second sheep, a second tasmiya is necessary, such that if he laid down two sheep one on top of the other and slaughtered them with one knife, they are permissible with one tasmiya. And if he laid down a sheep to slaughter it, then threw away that knife and took another knife, there is no harm, contrary to the case if he took an arrow. And he said tasmiya, then put that down and took another and did not say tasmiya, its consumption is permissible because in optional slaughter the tasmiya is prescribed on the slaughtering act, not on the tool, and the slaughtered animal has not changed. In emergency slaughter, the tasmiya is on the tool, not on the animal, and the tool changed. And from Abu Yusuf: If he laid down a sheep and said tasmiya, then released it and took another and slaughtered it with that tasmiya, it is not valid. And if he shot at game and hit another, it is permissible as we explained. And if he said tasmiya and then occupied himself with another, if it was a little like speaking to someone or drinking water, it is permissible; if it was lengthy, then no, because the act of slaughter is connected to the tasmiya such that nothing intervenes between them, and it is not possible except with a wound, so the session is considered as connection, and a small action does not break the session, so it is slaughtered with tasmiya, while a long action breaks and separates between them, so it is slaughtered without tasmiya. And if he said 'Allah', it is valid whether he intended or not, because it is explicit in tasmiya, and his apparent state indicates that he intended the tasmiya for the slaughter, so it occurs unless he diverts it. Even if he intended it for something else, like one who says 'Allahu Akbar' intending to respond to the adhan and not begin prayer, he does not become commencing it. And if he said 'Subhan Allah' or 'Alhamdulillah' or 'Allahu Akbar' intending thereby the tasmiya for the slaughter, it is permissible, otherwise no, because these words are metaphorical for tasmiya, and the metaphor takes the place of the explicit only with intention. And if he said 'Allah' without the 'ha' of 'Allah', if he intended tasmiya it is permissible, otherwise no, because the Arabs may drop a letter for brevity. So in al-Muhit. And in al-Tatimma: A man slaughtered and said 'Bismillah' it is valid whether he intended or not.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This long passage provides various rulings concerning tasmiya (mentioning Allah's name) during slaughter. Key points: 1) Two sheep: if you only say tasmiya on the first, only the first is halal. 2) Shooting multiple game with one arrow: one tasmiya suffices for all if they are hit simultaneously. 3) Two sheep placed together and slaughtered with one cut: one tasmiya suffices. 4) Changing the knife: permissible, but changing the arrow in emergency slaughter (where tasmiya is on the tool) may require new tasmiya. 5) Abu Yusuf's view: if you tasmiya on one sheep then release it and slaughter another with the same tasmiya, invalid. 6) If you shoot an arrow intended for one but it hits another, it's halal. 7) If you make tasmiya then do a minor action (speak, drink) the tasmiya remains valid; if a long gap, invalid. 8) Saying merely 'Allah' without 'Bismillah' suffices regardless of intention because it is explicit in tasmiya. 9) Using other words like 'Subhan Allah' requires intention of tasmiya. 10) Saying 'Allah' without the final 'h' (i.e., 'Alla') also requires intention. The passage emphasizes that in optional slaughter the tasmiya is on the act, not the tool, so changing tools doesn't break it; in emergency slaughter it's on the tool. Finally, from al-Tatimma, simply saying 'Bismillah' is valid with or without intention.

Key Terms
تسمية (tasmiya): The act of saying 'Bismillah' or equivalent at slaughter.
ذكاة (dhabh): The ritual slaughter of an animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter shoots an arrow at a flock and kills five birds, having said Bismillah. Are all five halal?
A) Yes, one tasmiya suffices because the act is one.
B) No, each bird needs its own tasmiya.
C) Only if the arrow hits all at once.
D) No, because it is emergency slaughter.

Key Takeaway
One tasmiya suffices for multiple animals killed by one act; separate acts require separate tasmiya.

Changing Tools and the Difference Between Optional and Emergency Slaughter
Ruling on tasmiya when changing tools in optional vs. emergency slaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why changing the knife does not require a new tasmiya but changing the arrow does, based on the distinction between optional (ikhtiyari) and emergency (idtirari) slaughter.

Core Idea
In optional slaughter, tasmiya is on the act, not the tool; in emergency slaughter, it is on the tool.

When slaughtering an animal, if you change the knife after saying tasmiya, the slaughter remains valid without repeating tasmiya. But if you change the arrow during emergency hunting, a new tasmiya is needed. This difference stems from the nature of the slaughter: optional slaughter focuses on the act, while emergency slaughter ties the tasmiya to the tool. The source passage clarifies this principle.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish ikhtiyari (optional) and idtirari (emergency) slaughter
Optional slaughter is the normal method of cutting the throat with a knife. Emergency slaughter is when the animal is killed by a projectile (e.g., arrow) in hunting.
The ruling on tasmiya differs between these two types.
2
Apply the principle to changing the knife
In optional slaughter, tasmiya is prescribed on the slaughtering act, not on the tool. So even if you change the knife after saying tasmiya, the act remains connected; no new tasmiya.
The animal has not changed; the tool is irrelevant.
3
Apply to changing the arrow
In emergency slaughter, tasmiya is on the tool (the arrow). So if you put down the arrow and take another without saying tasmiya again, the slaughter is invalid because the tool changed.
The text says 'contrary to the case if he took an arrow'.

Continuity Between Tasmiya and the Slaughter Act
Rulings on allowable gaps between tasmiya and the slaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that minor actions between tasmiya and slaughter do not invalidate the tasmiya, but a long gap does.

Core Idea
Tasmiya remains valid if the gap to slaughter consists of a minor action that does not break the 'session'.

After saying tasmiya, one must proceed to slaughter without a significant interruption. The Hanafi school uses the concept of 'majlis' (session) to determine what breaks the connection. Minor actions like speaking or drinking do not break the session; long gaps do. The source passage also addresses a related scenario from Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle of connection (ittisal)
The slaughter act must be connected to the tasmiya. A gap that breaks the connection requires a new tasmiya.
The 'majlis' (session) is the standard: as long as the person remains in the same session, minor interruptions are tolerated.
2
Apply to minor vs. major gaps
Speaking to someone or drinking water is a minor action that does not break the session. Lengthy actions such as walking away or engaging in a different task break the session.
The text contrasts 'little' (qalil) and 'lengthy' (tawil).
3
Consider Abu Yusuf's opinion
Abu Yusuf says if you say tasmiya on one sheep, release it, and then slaughter another with that tasmiya, it is invalid because the intended animal changed and there is a break.
This is a stricter view; the main position seems to focus on the same animal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ذبح شاتين فسمى على الأولى دون الثانية تحل الأولى دون الثانية، ولو سهماً إلى صيود فأثخن الكل يكفيه تسمية واحدة وإن حصل بها ذكاة صيود كثيرة، فأما ذبح الشاة الثانية فلا بد له من تسمية ثانية حتى لو أضجع شاتين إحداهما على الأخرى وذبحهما بحديدة يحلان بتسمية واحدة ولو أضجع شاة ليذبحها ثم ألقى تلك السكين وأخذ سكيناً أخرى لا بأس به بخلاف ما لو أخذ سهماً . وسمى فوضع ذلك ورفع آخر ولم يسم يحل أكله لأن التسمية في الذكاة الاختيارية مشروعة على الذبح لا على آلته والذبيحة لم تتغير، الذكاة الاضطرارية التسمية على الآلة لا على الذبيحة والآلة قد تغيرت. وعن أبي يوسف: ولو أضجع شاة وسمى فأرسلها وأخذ غيرها وذبحها بتلك التسمية لم تجز، ولو رمى إلى صيد فأصاب آخر يحل لما بينا . سمى واشتغل بآخر إن كان قليلاً كما لو كلم إنساناً أو شرب ماء يحل وإن كان طويلاً فلا لأن إيقاع الذبح متصل بالتسمية بحيث لا يتخلل بينهما شيء ولا يمكن إلا بجرح فأقيم المجلس مقام الإتصال والعمل القليل لا يقطع المجلس فيكون مذبوحاً على التسمية والكثير يقطع فيفصل بينهما فيكون مذبوحاً بغير تسمية. ولو قال الله جاز نوى أو لم ينو لأنه صريح في التسمية، وظاهر حاله يدل على أنه أراد به التسمية على الذبيحة فيقع عنها ما لم يوجد منه الصرف عنها حتى لو أراد به التسمية على غيره كمن قال الله أكبر وأراد به إجابة الأذان لا افتتاح الصلاة ولم يصر شارعاً فيها. ولو سبح أو حمد الله أو كبر يريد به التسمية على الذبيحة تحل وإلا فلا لأن هذه الألفاظ كناية عن والكناية إنما تقوم مقام الصريح بالنية. ولو قال الله بغير هاء الله إن أراد به التسمية يحل وإلا فلا لأن العرب قد تحذف حرفاً ترخيماً؛ كذا في المحيط. وفي التتمة: رجل ذبح وفي بسم الله جاز نوى أو لم ينو.
And if he slaughtered two sheep and said tasmiya on the first but not the second, the first is permissible and the second is not. And if he shot an arrow at a group of game and it killed all of them, one tasmiya is sufficient for all, even if it made many games lawful. As for slaughtering the second sheep, a second tasmiya is necessary, such that if he laid down two sheep one on top of the other and slaughtered them with one knife, they are permissible with one tasmiya. And if he laid down a sheep to slaughter it, then threw away that knife and took another knife, there is no harm, contrary to the case if he took an arrow. And he said tasmiya, then put that down and took another and did not say tasmiya, its consumption is permissible because in optional slaughter the tasmiya is prescribed on the slaughtering act, not on the tool, and the slaughtered animal has not changed. In emergency slaughter, the tasmiya is on the tool, not on the animal, and the tool changed. And from Abu Yusuf: If he laid down a sheep and said tasmiya, then released it and took another and slaughtered it with that tasmiya, it is not valid. And if he shot at game and hit another, it is permissible as we explained. And if he said tasmiya and then occupied himself with another, if it was a little like speaking to someone or drinking water, it is permissible; if it was lengthy, then no, because the act of slaughter is connected to the tasmiya such that nothing intervenes between them, and it is not possible except with a wound, so the session is considered as connection, and a small action does not break the session, so it is slaughtered with tasmiya, while a long action breaks and separates between them, so it is slaughtered without tasmiya. And if he said 'Allah', it is valid whether he intended or not, because it is explicit in tasmiya, and his apparent state indicates that he intended the tasmiya for the slaughter, so it occurs unless he diverts it. Even if he intended it for something else, like one who says 'Allahu Akbar' intending to respond to the adhan and not begin prayer, he does not become commencing it. And if he said 'Subhan Allah' or 'Alhamdulillah' or 'Allahu Akbar' intending thereby the tasmiya for the slaughter, it is permissible, otherwise no, because these words are metaphorical for tasmiya, and the metaphor takes the place of the explicit only with intention. And if he said 'Allah' without the 'ha' of 'Allah', if he intended tasmiya it is permissible, otherwise no, because the Arabs may drop a letter for brevity. So in al-Muhit. And in al-Tatimma: A man slaughtered and said 'Bismillah' it is valid whether he intended or not.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This long passage provides various rulings concerning tasmiya (mentioning Allah's name) during slaughter. Key points: 1) Two sheep: if you only say tasmiya on the first, only the first is halal. 2) Shooting multiple game with one arrow: one tasmiya suffices for all if they are hit simultaneously. 3) Two sheep placed together and slaughtered with one cut: one tasmiya suffices. 4) Changing the knife: permissible, but changing the arrow in emergency slaughter (where tasmiya is on the tool) may require new tasmiya. 5) Abu Yusuf's view: if you tasmiya on one sheep then release it and slaughter another with the same tasmiya, invalid. 6) If you shoot an arrow intended for one but it hits another, it's halal. 7) If you make tasmiya then do a minor action (speak, drink) the tasmiya remains valid; if a long gap, invalid. 8) Saying merely 'Allah' without 'Bismillah' suffices regardless of intention because it is explicit in tasmiya. 9) Using other words like 'Subhan Allah' requires intention of tasmiya. 10) Saying 'Allah' without the final 'h' (i.e., 'Alla') also requires intention. The passage emphasizes that in optional slaughter the tasmiya is on the act, not the tool, so changing tools doesn't break it; in emergency slaughter it's on the tool. Finally, from al-Tatimma, simply saying 'Bismillah' is valid with or without intention.

Key Terms
مجلس (majlis): The session in which the tasmiya and slaughter occur; used as a measure of continuity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After saying Bismillah, the butcher drinks a glass of water and then slaughters. Is the slaughter valid?
A) Valid, because drinking is a minor action
B) Invalid, because any action after tasmiya breaks it
C) Valid, only if he intended the drink as part of the slaughter
D) Invalid, because drinking is lengthy

Key Takeaway
Minor actions like speaking or drinking do not break the connection between tasmiya and slaughter; lengthy actions do.

Acceptable Verbal Formulas for Tasmiya
Rulings on verbal formulas for tasmiya: explicit vs. metaphorical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which verbal formulas suffice for tasmiya and when intention is required.

Core Idea
Saying "Allah" or "Bismillah" is explicit; other phrases like "Subhan Allah" require intention of tasmiya.

The tasmiya in slaughter can be performed using various words. The Hanafi school distinguishes between explicit phrases that inherently indicate tasmiya, and metaphorical phrases that require the person's intention. The source passage provides detailed examples, including saying "Allah" alone, dropping the final 'ha', and using tasbih or takbir.

Guided Path
1
Identify explicit vs. metaphorical phrases
Phrases like "Allah" and "Bismillah" are considered explicit (sarih) for tasmiya, so they are valid with or without intention. Other phrases like "Subhan Allah", "Alhamdulillah", "Allahu Akbar" are metaphorical (kinayah) and require intention of tasmiya.
Explicit: the word itself directly indicates invoking Allah's name. Metaphorical: general praise that could be for other reasons.
2
Apply to 'Allah' alone
Saying just "Allah" (without 'Bismillah') is considered explicit for tasmiya because the apparent state indicates the purpose. It is valid even without explicit intention.
The text says 'it is valid whether he intended or not because it is explicit in tasmiya.'
3
Apply to 'Allah' without the ha (Alla)
If one says 'Alla' (dropping the ha) for brevity, it is valid only if intended for tasmiya; otherwise not, because it is not the full word and could be a casual exclamation.
Arabs sometimes drop letters; but without intention, it does not count.
4
Apply to other phrases
Saying 'Subhan Allah' or 'Alhamdulillah' requires intention of tasmiya; if not intended, it does not count as tasmiya.
These are general dhikr, so only with specific intention do they substitute.

Implied Permission (Ibaha Dhilalah) in Hanafi Fiqh
Rulings on actions based on inferred consent of owner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi concept of ibaha dhilalah to determine the permissibility of actions based on custom and inferred consent, and distinguish between possession (ihraz) and mere custody.

Core Idea
Permission is inferred from custom and context, not always explicit words.

In Hanafi fiqh, actions are often judged by what is customarily understood as permissible, even without explicit permission. This lesson examines four cases from Bahr al-Raiq where the ruling hinges on implied consent and the concept of ihraz (possession). Understanding ibaha dhilalah is crucial for navigating everyday transactions and property rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its apparent ruling
For each case, first state what action is being evaluated (e.g., dyeing hair, taking fruit, taking sugar, collecting water) and the initial ruling (permissible or not permissible).
The ruling in each case is mubah (permissible) or haram depending on context.
2
Determine the basis of implied permission
Ask: Is there a customary indicator or necessity that would lead a reasonable person to infer consent? For example, dyeing hair increases marital affection; fruits in a river spoil water if not removed.
The Hanafi school gives weight to custom (urf) and necessity (darura).
3
Analyze the concept of ihraz (possession)
In the sugar and basin cases, the key is whether the initial person had effectively taken possession (ihraz) of the object. Ihraz requires both physical control and intention to possess.
If the lap was intended as a receptacle, the person possessed the sugar; otherwise not.
4
Distinguish between intention and circumstance
The owner's intention when placing the basin determines ownership of the water. If placed to collect water, the water belongs to the owner; otherwise, the collector acquires it.
Intent is inferred from action and custom.
5
Recognize the Hanafi principle of ibaha dhilalah
The common thread is that permission is derived from the context and general custom, not an explicit statement. This is broader than some other madhhabs.
This principle is related to the maxim 'al-urf muhakkam' (custom is authoritative).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتح الرأس واللحية بالحناء والوشمة للرجال والنساء لأن ذلك سبب لزيادة الرغبة والمحبة بين الزوجين. ويجوز رفع الثمار من نهر جار وأكلها وإن كثر لأنه مما يفسد الماء إذا ترك فيكون مأذوناً بالرفع دلالة. رجل نثر السكر فوقع في حجر رجل فأخذه رجل آخر منه إن كان حجره ليقع فيه السكر لا يجوز لأنه أحرزه وإلا فيجوز لأنه ما أحرزه، ونظيره رجل وضع طشتاً على سطح فاجتمع فيه ماء المطر فجاء رجل ورفعه إن كان وضعه صاحبه لذلك فهو له، وإن لم يضعه لذلك فهو للرافع لأنه لم يحرزه
Opening the head and beard with henna and dyeing for men and women because that is a cause for increase of desire and love between spouses. And it is permissible to raise fruits from a flowing river and eat them, even if many, because they spoil the water if left, so permission to raise them is inferred. A man threw sugar and it fell into a man's lap, then another man took it from him: if his lap was intended for the sugar to fall into, it is not permissible because he has possessed it; otherwise it is permissible because he has not possessed it. And its analogy is a man who placed a basin on a roof and rainwater collected in it; another man came and raised it: if its owner placed it for that purpose, it is his; if he did not place it for that purpose, it belongs to the raiser because he has not possessed it.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage presents rulings on implied permission (ibaha dhilalah). The first ruling: dyeing hair and beard with henna for both men and women is permissible because it enhances marital affection. Second: taking fruits from a flowing river is allowed because they would otherwise spoil the water. Third: if sugar falls into someone's lap, another person cannot take it if the lap was intended as a receptacle; otherwise it is free. Fourth: rainwater collecting in a basin left on a roof belongs to the owner if placed for that purpose; otherwise to the one who collects it. The reasoning is based on the concept of ihraz (possession) and intention.

Key Terms
إباحة دلالة (ibaha dhilalah): Permission inferred from circumstances and custom, not explicit statement
إحراز (ihraz): Possession that establishes ownership or exclusive right

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a basin placed on a roof collecting rainwater, when does the water belong to the basin owner according to the passage?
A) Always, because the basin is his property
B) Only if he placed the basin with the intention of collecting water
C) Only if he explicitly says he wants the water
D) Never, because rainwater is ownerless

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, permission for an action can be inferred from custom and necessity, and possession (ihraz) requires both physical control and intention.

Eating Fallen Fruits: Presumption of Consent for Perishable Produce
Intra-Hanafi dispute on eating fallen fruits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for eating fallen fruits in different contexts and explain the rationale behind the preferred position for perishable fruits.

Core Idea
For fruits that spoil quickly, owner’s consent is presumed; for durable fruits, explicit permission is required.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a practical scenario: may one eat fruits that have fallen under trees without the owner's explicit permission? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on location, type of fruit, and custom. An intra-madhhab dispute arises regarding fruits that do not last, with al-Sadr al-Shahid's lenient view becoming the relied-upon position. Understanding this ruling helps navigate everyday interactions with private property in a Shariah-compliant manner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
The text first distinguishes between city (where explicit permission is needed) and summer heat (qayz). Then within summer, it separates fruits that last (like walnuts) from those that do not. Also note the special context of 'harasiyya' (fields). Finally, leaves are treated separately.
The city vs. countryside distinction relies on custom; in cities, permission is not presumed due to proximity to owner.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
For fruits that do not last, al-Sadr al-Shahid holds that taking is permissible unless the owner's prohibition is known (explicitly or by custom). Al-Utabiyya requires knowing the owner's pleasure. Identify the basis of each view: custom vs. strict property rights.
Do not confuse the two views; the text calls al-Sadr al-Shahid’s view 'al-mukhtar' in multiple sources.
3
Determine the mufta bihi
The passage states that al-Sadr al-Shahid's view is 'al-mukhtar' and thus the operative position for fatwa. This is reinforced by the mention of al-Ghiyathiyya and the exception for harasiyya.
Even where there is ikhtilaf, the Hanafi school often adopts the more lenient position when it aligns with custom.
4
Apply the ruling to carrying and leaves
Jami' al-Jawami' prohibits carrying any fallen fruit. For leaves, the ruling depends on benefit: if the leaves are useful (e.g., mulberry), taking is prohibited and guarantees; if not useful, taking is allowed without guarantee.
Carrying is a separate act from eating; the prohibition on carrying may be due to taking larger amounts.
5
Connect to the broader fiqh principles
This case illustrates the Hanafi reliance on custom (urf) and presumption of consent in property matters, especially when the owner would likely permit. Contrast with the stricter position that emphasizes the sanctity of ownership.
The key maxim: 'Custom is considered a source of law' (al-‘aadah muhakkamah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر في عيون المسائل إذا ... الرجل بالثمار في أيام الصيف وأراد أن يتناول منها الثمار الساقطة تحت الأشجار، فإن كان ذلك في المصر لا يسعه التناول إلا إذا علم أن صاحبها قد أباح إما نصاً أو دلالة أو عادة، وإذا كان في القيظ فإن كان من الثمار التي تبقى مثل الجوز وغيره لا يسعه الأخذ إلا إذا علم الإذن . وفي الغياثية : هو المختار . وإن كان من الثمار التي لا تبقى اختلفوا فيه ؛ قال الصدر الشهيد : والمختار أنه لا بأس بالتناول ما لم يتبين النهي إما صريحاً أو عادة وفي العتابية : والمختار أنه لا يأكل منهما ما لم يعلم أن صاحبها رضي بذلك وإن كان ذلك في الوسواس التي يقال لها بالفارسية هراسية، فإن كان من الثمار التي لا تبقى فالمختار أنه لا بأس بالأكل ما لم يتبين النهي. وفي جامع الجوامع ولا يحل حمل شيء منه، وأما إذا كان الثمار على الأشجار فالأفضل أن لا يؤخذ في موضع ما إلا أن يأذن أو يكون موضع كثير الثمار يعلم أنه لا يشق عليه أكل ذلك فيسعه الأكل ولا يسعه الحمل. وأما أوراق الأشجار إذا سقط على الطريق في أيام العيلق وأخذ إنسان شيئاً من ذلك بغير إذن صاحب الشجر ؛ فإن كان هذا ورق شجر ينتفع بورقه نحو التوت وما أشبه ذلك ليس له أن يأخذ وإن أخذ يضمن، وإن كان لا ينتفع به له أن يأخذ وإن أخذ لا يضمن.
And it is mentioned in Uyun al-Masa'il: If a man [is] with fruits in summer days and wants to take from the fruits falling under trees: if that is in the city, it is not permissible for him to take unless he knows that the owner has permitted, either explicitly, by indication, or by custom. And if it is in the heat [of summer], then if it is from fruits that last like walnuts and others, it is not permissible for him to take unless he knows permission. In al-Ghiyathiyya: That is the chosen opinion. And if it is from fruits that do not last, they differed about it. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said: The chosen opinion is that there is no harm in taking as long as prohibition is not evident, either explicitly or by custom. And in al-Utabiyya: The chosen opinion is that he should not eat from either unless he knows that the owner is pleased with that. And if that is in the [؟] which is called in Persian harasiyya, then if it is from fruits that do not last, the chosen opinion is that there is no harm in eating as long as prohibition is not evident. And in Jami' al-Jawami': It is not permissible to carry any of it. As for fruits on trees, the best is not to take in any place unless he permits, or it is a place with many fruits and he knows that it does not harm him to eat that; then it is permissible for him to eat but not to carry. As for leaves of trees that fall on the road in the days of [؟], and a person takes something from that without permission of the owner: if this is leaves of a tree that benefit from its leaves like mulberry and the like, he may not take and if he takes, he guarantees; if it does not benefit, he may take and if he takes, he does not guarantee.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the permissibility of eating fallen fruits under trees. The initial ruling distinguishes between city and countryside: in the city, one cannot take fallen fruits without knowing the owner's permission. In the heat of summer, a distinction is made based on the type of fruit. For fruits that last (like walnuts), permission is required. For fruits that do not last, there is a difference of opinion. Al-Sadr al-Shahid's preferred view is that it is permissible unless the owner's prohibition is known (either explicit or by custom). Al-Utabiyya's preferred view is stricter: one should not eat unless he knows the owner is pleased. The text also notes a specific context ('في الوسواس' or 'هراسية') where the more lenient view is chosen. Jami' al-Jawami' adds that carrying any fallen fruit is not permissible. For fruits still on trees, the best is not to take without permission, but if the location is abundant and eating does not harm, one may eat but not carry. For fallen leaves, the ruling depends on whether the leaves are beneficial: if beneficial (like mulberry), taking is prohibited and requires guarantee; if not beneficial, taking is allowed without guarantee.

Key Terms
القيظ (al-qayẓ): The intense summer heat; the season when fruits fall.
المختار (al-mukhtār): The chosen or preferred opinion among the Hanafi scholars.
العتابية (al-ʿutābiyyah): A work of Hanafi fiqh by al-ʿUtābi; a source for the stricter view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Sadr al-Shahid, what is the ruling on eating fallen fruits that do not last?
A) It is never permissible without explicit permission
B) It is permissible as long as the owner's prohibition is not known
C) It is permissible only if the owner is pleased
D) It is forbidden in all cases

Key Takeaway
For perishable fallen fruits, the Hanafi school presumes the owner's consent unless prohibition is known, overruling the stricter requirement of explicit permission.
