Accepting a Non-Muslim's Statement in Transactions: Implicit Acceptance (Dhimni)
Passage explaining the correction of the dropped word 'dhimni' and the necessity-based acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts a non-Muslim's statement in transactions and distinguish between direct and implicit (dhimni) rulings.

Core Idea
A non-Muslim’s statement is accepted in transactions, and implicitly in halal/haram, due to necessity.

This lesson clarifies a common confusion: did the author say a non-Muslim's statement is accepted in halal and haram? The correct understanding is that it is accepted implicitly (dhimni) as a necessary consequence of accepting it in the transaction itself. We will unpack the reasoning and the analogy that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim and the correction
The author wrote that a non-Muslim's statement regarding halal and haram is accepted. The commentator notes this is a mistake unless the word 'implicitly' (dhimni) is added, which was dropped by scribes.
The correction is crucial to understanding the Hanafi position.
2
Understand the necessity (darurah)
The Hanafi school accepts it in transactions because transactions are frequent and people rely on others; rejecting such reports would cause hardship.
Necessity overrides general prohibition.
3
Grasp the concept of dhimni (implicit)
Something can be valid implicitly even if not valid directly. Example: sale of water rights alone is invalid, but valid when accompanying land sale.
The analogy illustrates the principle.
4
Apply to the scenario
When a non-Muslim servant buys meat, his statement about the purchase is accepted, and this implies acceptance about its permissibility (halal/haram) as a necessary consequence.
The implicit acceptance covers halal/haram only in context of the transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ويقبل قول الكافر في الحل والحرمة) قال الشارح وهذا . سهو لأن الحل والحرمة من الديانات ولا يقبل قول الكافر في الديانات وإنما يقبل قوله في المعاملات خاصة للضرورة لأن خبره صحيح لصدوره عن عقل ودين يعتقد فيه حرمة الكذب والحاجة ماسة إلى قبول قوله لكثرة وقوع المعاملات ا.هـ. أقول : أن أصل عبارة المؤلف في الحل والحرمة لضمني فأسقط بعض الكتبة لفظ الضمني فشاع ذلك واشتهر حتى إذا كان خادم كافر أو أجير مجوسي فأرسله ليشتري له لحماً فقال اشتريت من يهودي أو نصراني أو مسلم و وسعه أكله، وإن قال اشتريت من مجوسي يسعه فعله لأنه لما قبل قوله في حق الشراء منه لزم قبوله في حق الحل والحرمة ضرورة لما ذكرنا وإن كان لا يقبل قوله فيه قصداً بأن قال حلال أو هذا حرام ألا ترى أن بـ بيع الشرب وحده لا يجوز وتبعاً للأرض يجوز وكم من شيء يصح ضمناً وإن لم يصح قصداً؛ كذا صرحوا به قاطبة ولو قال اشتريته من غير المسلم والكتابي فإنه يقبل قوله في ذلك ويتضمن حرمة ما اشتراه كما صرحوا به أيضاً . قال رحمه الله : (والمملوك والصبي في الهدية والإذن) والأصل أن المعاملات يقبل فيها خبر كل مميز حراً كان أو عبداً مسلماً كان أو كافراً، صغيراً كان أو كبيراً لعموم الضرورة الداعية إلى ذلك وإلى سقوط اشتراط العدالة فإن الإنسان قلما يجد المستجمع لشرائط العدالة ولا دليل مع السامع يعمل به سوى الخبر فلو لم يقبل خبره لامتنع باب المعاملات ووقعوا في حرج عظيم وبابه مفتوح، ولأن المعاملات ليس فيها إلزام واشتراط العدالة للإلزام فلا معنى لاشتراطها فيها فاشترط فيها التمييز لا غير فإذا قبل فيها قول المميز وكان في ضمن قبوله فيها قبوله في الديانات يقبل قوله في الديانات ضمناً لما
He (the author) said: (And the statement of the disbeliever regarding halal and haram is accepted). The commentator said: This is a mistake, because halal and haram are matters of religion, and the statement of a disbeliever is not accepted in religious matters; rather, his statement is accepted in transactions specifically out of necessity, because his report is valid as it originates from intellect and a religion in which he believes lying is forbidden, and need is pressing to accept his statement due to the frequency of transactions. End. I say: The original phrase of the author was 'in halal and haram implicitly' (dhimni), so some scribes dropped the word 'implicitly', and it became widespread and famous. So if there is a disbelieving servant or a Magian hired worker, and he sends him to buy meat, and he says 'I bought it from a Jew or Christian or Muslim', he is allowed to eat it. And if he said 'I bought it from a Magian', he is allowed to do so, because since his statement is accepted regarding the purchase itself, it necessarily implies acceptance regarding halal and haram due to necessity as we mentioned, even though his statement is not accepted directly if he said 'it is halal' or 'this is haram'. Do you not see that the sale of water-drinking alone is not valid, but as a follow-up to land it is valid? And many things are valid implicitly even if not valid directly. They all explicitly stated this. And if he said 'I bought it from a non-Muslim and non-Scriptuary', his statement is accepted in that and implies the prohibition of what he bought, as they also explicitly stated. He (the author) said: (And the slave and the minor regarding gifts and permission). The principle is that in transactions, the report of every discerning person is accepted, whether free or slave, Muslim or disbeliever, young or old, due to the general necessity leading to that and to dropping the condition of probity ('adalah). For a person rarely finds someone who fulfills the conditions of probity, and the listener has no evidence to act upon except the report. So if his report were not accepted, the category of transactions would be prevented, and they would fall into great hardship, and its door is open. And because transactions do not involve compulsion, and probity is required for compulsion, so there is no meaning to stipulating it in them. Therefore discrimination (tamyiz) alone is stipulated. So if the statement of a discerning person is accepted in transactions, and it includes implicitly acceptance in religious matters, his statement is accepted in religious matters implicitly.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage addresses the rule of accepting statements from non-Muslims, slaves, and children in the context of transactions. The author initially wrote that a disbeliever's statement regarding halal and haram is accepted, but the commentator corrects that this is only implicitly. The original text had the word 'implicitly' which was dropped by copyists. In Hanafi fiqh, necessity requires accepting the word of any discriminating person in transactional matters, because transactions are frequent and a reliable Muslim witness is often unavailable. The acceptance is for the transaction itself (e.g., 'I bought this meat'), and it necessarily implies the ruling of the item's permissibility (halal) or prohibition (haram) as a necessary consequence (dhimni). Similarly, slaves and minors are accepted in transactions like gifts and permissions, because the condition is simply discernment (tamyiz), not probity ('adalah). The reasoning is that probity is required only in matters involving compulsion (like testimony in court), but transactions are not compulsory. The examples of sale of water rights as part of land illustrate that something can be valid implicitly even if not valid independently.

Key Terms
ضمني (dhimni): Implicit or incidental; acceptance of a ruling as a necessary consequence of another accepted matter.
المعاملات (al-mu'amalat): Transactions, such as buying, selling, gifts, etc., as opposed to pure religious testimonies (shahadat).
التمييز (al-tamyiz): Discernment; the ability to understand the nature of the transaction and its implications.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the commentator in the passage claim the author's original statement was a 'mistake'?
A) Because non-Muslims are never truthful.
B) Because the word 'implicitly' (dhimni) was dropped, making it seem like direct acceptance in religious matters.
C) Because the author was wrong about everything.
D) Because it contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts a non-Muslim's statement in transactions and implicitly in halal/haram due to necessity and the principle of dhimni acceptance.

Accepting Statements of Slaves and Minors in Transactions: Tamyiz Over 'Adalah
Passage stating the general rule for accepting reports in transactions from any discerning person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for accepting a person's statement in transactions and apply it to slaves and minors.

Core Idea
In transactions, any discerning person's report is accepted; probity is not required.

The Hanafi school extends the same necessity-based acceptance to slaves and children. The key requirement is discernment (tamyiz), not moral probity ('adalah). We will explore the reasoning that underlies this and see how it keeps transactions flowing without undue hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The author states that in transactions, the report of every discerning person is accepted, whether free or slave, Muslim or disbeliever, young or old.
This is a comprehensive rule.
2
Understand the necessity (darurah)
The reason is that transactions are frequent and it is hard to find a person with full probity; rejecting reports would cause great hardship.
Necessity is the driving force.
3
Distinguish from compulsion-based testimony
Probity ('adalah) is required only for matters involving compulsion (like court testimony), but transactions are not compulsory.
This distinction justifies the relaxed requirement.
4
Determine the condition
Only tamyiz (discernment) is required; 'adalah (probity) is dropped.
Discernment means understanding the nature of the transaction.
5
Apply to the rulings
Slaves and minors are accepted in gifts and permissions (e.g., a slave can give a gift with permission). Their report is also implicitly accepted in religious matters as a consequence.
The implicit acceptance follows the same dhimni principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Touching a Female Slave for Purchase: Permissibility and Limits
Permissibility of touching a female slave for purchase with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on touching a female slave for purchase and identify the conditions that make it permissible.

Core Idea
Touching a female slave for purchase is permissible due to necessity, even if desire is present.

When buying a female slave, the prospective buyer needs to assess her physical condition. In the Hanafi school, this necessity (darurah) makes permissible what would otherwise be prohibited: the buyer may touch certain parts of her body, even if he experiences desire. This lesson explains the ruling, the scope of permitted touching, and how it applies to other categories of slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Recognize that touching a non-mahram woman is normally prohibited. The act under discussion is touching a female slave with the intention to purchase.
The default ruling (before necessity) is haram.
2
Identify the exception: necessity (darurah)
The matn states that touching is permissible when the buyer intends to buy. The reason is necessity—the need to assess the slave for purchase overrides the prohibition.
Necessity makes permissible what is otherwise prohibited.
3
List the parts that may be touched
The commentary specifies: chest, leg, forearm, head, and hair. Only parts that are normally viewable can be touched; private parts remain prohibited.
The scope is limited to what is necessary for assessment.
4
Understand the effect of desire
The matn explicitly says 'even if he desires.' The presence of desire does not negate the permissibility because the necessity (purchase) is the controlling factor.
This distinguishes the Hanafi position from stricter views.
5
Extend the ruling to similar categories
The commentary extends the same ruling to umm al-walad, mudabbara, and mukataba because they are still slaves. For mustas'at, Abu Hanifa treats her like mukataba.
The ruling is based on the existence of slavery and the need to assess.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وله مس ذلك إذا أراد الشراء وإن اشتهى) يعني جاز له أن يمس كل موضع بجوز له أن ينظر إليه كالصدر والساق والذراع والرأس ويقلب شعرها إذا أراد الشراء وإن خاف على الشراء فيباح له النظر والمس للضرورة وهو إرادة الشراء. وفي الشارح: أمة الرجل تكبس رجل زوجها ويخلو بها ولا يمنع من ذلك أحد، وأم الولد والمتدبرة والمكاتبة كالأمة لقيام الرق فيهن ووجود الحاجة والمستسعاة كالمكاتبة عند الإمام .
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And he may touch that when he intends to buy even if he desires). Meaning: It is permissible for him to touch any place that it is permissible for him to look at, like the chest, leg, forearm, and head, and to turn over her hair when he intends to buy, even if he desires. The looking and touching are permitted due to necessity, which is the intention to buy. In al-Sharih: The female slave of a man may press the leg of her husband? Or the text: 'amat al-rajli tukabbisu rajla zawjiha' meaning she may massage? Actually 'tekbisu' may be a typo; it means she may rub the leg of her husband and be alone with him without anyone preventing. And the umm al-walad, mudabbara, and mukataba are like the female slave due to the existence of slavery and need, and the mustas'at (one who is given a contract to earn freedom) is like the mukatab according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage explains the matn ruling that when a man intends to buy a female slave, he is permitted to touch parts that he would normally be allowed to look at, such as chest, leg, forearm, head, and even handle her hair. This is despite the fact that she is a non-mahram and even if he feels desire. The reasoning is that the purchase requires assessment, and necessity makes permissible what would otherwise be forbidden. The commentary adds a related ruling: someone else's female slave is allowed to rub the leg of her husband (the master's husband?) and be alone with him, and no one can prevent that. Furthermore, the same ruling applies to an umm al-walad (mother of his child), mudabbara (a slave promising freedom after master's death), and mukataba (slave with contract to earn freedom), because they are still slaves and the need exists. However, the mustas'at (an insolvent slave trying to earn freedom) is treated like a mukataba according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
ضرورة (ḍarūrah): Necessity that makes permissible what is otherwise prohibited; here, the need to assess the slave for purchase justifies touching.
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, thereby gaining certain rights and eventually freedom upon master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on touching a female slave's hair when intending to buy her?
A) Haram, because she is a non-mahram.
B) Permissible, because purchase necessity allows it even if he desires.
C) Permissible only if he does not feel desire.
D) Makruh, because it leads to fitna.

Key Takeaway
Touching a female slave for purchase is mubah due to necessity, even if desire is present, but limited to normally viewable parts.

Ruling on Not Displaying a Mature Female Slave in a Single Garment
Prohibition on displaying a mature female slave in a single izār.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on displaying a mature female slave for sale in a single izār and identify which parts of the body must be covered.

Core Idea
A mature female slave must not be displayed in one garment – her back and belly are 'awrah that must be concealed.

This lesson addresses a specific ruling in sales transactions: when a seller intends to offer a female slave for sale, he must dress her properly. The source text prohibits displaying her in a single izār if she has reached puberty or the age of desire, because that leaves her back and belly exposed, which are considered 'awrah. We will examine the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is displaying a female slave for sale. The ruling is a prohibition (makruh tahrimi) if she is mature and displayed in a single izār.
The text uses 'la ta'rid' (do not display).
2
Define the izār
The izār is defined as a garment covering from navel to knee. This is not sufficient for a mature female slave.
The commentary specifies that the izār alone leaves the back and belly exposed.
3
Identify the 'awrah
The back and belly of the female slave are considered 'awrah and must be covered. The text explicitly states that it is not permissible to expose them.
'Awrah is the part of the body that must be concealed.
4
Note the additional category
The ruling applies not only to those who have reached puberty but also to any female who has reached the age of desire (shahwah), even if not physically mature.
This includes prepubescent girls who are objects of desire.
5
Identify the source
The ruling is narrated from Muhammad al-Shaybani, indicating its authority in the Hanafi school.
The text says 'ruwiya dhalika 'an Muhammad'.

Non-Muslim Property Rights: Using Owned Land as a Cemetery
A fatwa affirming non-Muslim property rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on non-Muslims using owned property as a cemetery and identify the underlying principle that ownership grants the same rights as Muslims.

Core Idea
Ownership grants the right to use property as one wishes, regardless of the owner's religion.

This brief passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq answers a practical question: can Jews who purchase property in a Muslim land be prevented from turning it into a cemetery? The ruling hinges on a fundamental principle of property rights in Islamic law. We will unpack the reasoning and understand how ownership overrides religious differences in this context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
What is the specific question being asked? A group of Jews bought a house and garden from Muslims in Egypt and want to use it as a cemetery. Should they be stopped?
The property was legally purchased from Muslim owners.
2
State the ruling
The scholar (likely Ibn Nujaym) answers: No, they are not to be prevented. The ruling is clear and unconditional.
The ruling is mubah (permissible) for the owners.
3
Extract the legal basis
The reason given is 'because they own it' (liannahum malakuhā). Ownership is the decisive factor. They are free to do as they wish (yaf'alūn mā shā'ū).
No reference to permission from authorities or neighborhood consent.
4
Apply the principle broadly
The ruling analogizes to Muslim owners: they are like Muslims. This implies that ownership rights in Hanafi law are equal for all legal residents, Muslim or dhimmi.
This does not apply to uses that are intrinsically haram (e.g., gambling den).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسئل عن قوم من اليهود اشتروا داراً وبستاناً من دور المسلمين في مصر واتخذوها مقبرة هل يمنعون من ذلك؟ فقال : لا لأنهم ملكوها فيفعلون ما شاءوا كالمسلمين.
He was asked about a group of Jews who bought a house and a garden from the houses of Muslims in Egypt and made it a cemetery; should they be prevented from that? He said: No, because they own it, so they do as they wish, like Muslims.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This is a straightforward ruling on private property rights for non-Muslims under Islamic rule. The question is whether Jews who purchase property (a house and garden) can convert it into a cemetery. The answer is that they cannot be prevented because they own the property; ownership grants them the same rights as Muslims to use the land as they see fit. This reflects the Hanafi principle that non-Muslims (dhimmis) have full rights of ownership and disposal over what they legitimately acquire, as long as they do not violate explicit Islamic law (e.g., building a church without permission). Here, making a cemetery is a permissible use.

Key Terms
ملك (milk): Ownership, the right to use and dispose of property as one wishes within legal limits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because it is dar al-Islam.
B) No, because they own it.
C) Only if it disturbs neighbors.
D) Only if it is near a mosque.

Key Takeaway
Ownership rights in Hanafi law are universal: what is permissible for a Muslim owner is permissible for a non-Muslim owner, provided the use is not inherently prohibited.

Property Owner's Right to Build Without Liability for Harm
Two rulings on property owner's right to build and the neighbor's inability to prevent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a property owner may build on his property even if it harms a neighbor, and distinguish this from actionable harm.

Core Idea
Owning property gives you the right to use it, even if it harms your neighbor.

This lesson examines two clear rulings from the Hanafi tradition: a house owner may raise a building or make openings that block sunlight or wind, and a neighbor cannot prevent someone from building higher on his own wall. The underlying principle is that acting within one's own property is not subject to a neighbor's veto, even if it causes some harm. However, later Hanafi scholars qualified this with conditions about excessive or malicious harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and rulings
The text gives two scenarios: (1) raising a building that blocks sun/wind, making holes/doors; (2) building higher on one's own wall. For each, the ruling is that the neighbor cannot prevent it.
Both rulings are based on the same principle.
2
Extract the legal principle
The reason given is 'because he has only acted within his own property'. This establishes the principle that lawful use of one's property is not actionable, even if it causes harm (darar) to a neighbor.
This is a general rule in Hanafi fiqh regarding neighborly relations.
3
Consider limits and exceptions
While the text is absolute, later Hanafi jurists introduced exceptions: if the harm is excessive or the act is purely malicious, the neighbor may have recourse. This lesson focuses on the basic ruling.
These exceptions are developed in other texts on darar and hisbah.
4
Compare with other schools
Other schools (e.g., Maliki) may require the owner to avoid harm or compensate. The Hanafi position is distinct in allowing harm from lawful use.
This ikhtilaf is important for understanding the school's approach to property rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد صحت الرواية في المبسوط أن صاحب الدرا لو رفع بناء فمنع جاءه الشمس أو الريح أو نقب جداره أو فتح أبواباً لم يمنع من ذلك وإن لحق جاره نوع ضرر لأنه لم يتصرف إلا في ملك نفسه. وسئل عن الرجل يبني على حائط نفسه بناء أزيد مما كان هل لجاره أن يمنعه؟ قال : لا وإن بلغ عنان السماء.
It is a sound narration in al-Mabsut that the owner of a house, if he raises a building and it blocks the sun or wind, or he makes a hole in his wall or opens doors, he is not prevented from that even if it causes some harm to his neighbor, because he has only acted within his own property. And he was asked about a man who builds on his own wall a structure higher than before; does his neighbor have the right to prevent him? He said: No, even if it reaches the sky.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents two related rulings on property rights. The first, from al-Mabsut, states that a property owner may build or modify his property even if it causes harm (e.g., blocking sunlight or wind, making openings) to a neighbor, because he is acting within his own domain. The second ruling reinforces this: a neighbor cannot prevent someone from building higher on his own wall, no matter how high. The principle is that ownership grants full authority over one's property, and harm to a neighbor is not a sufficient legal basis to stop the action, as the owner is only using his own property. This is subject to later qualifications in Hanafi law regarding pre-emption rights and excessive harm, but the text holds to the general principle.

Key Terms
ضرر (darar): Harm or damage to a neighbor's property, such as blocking light or air.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Can a neighbor prevent an owner from building higher on his own wall if it blocks sunlight?
A) Yes, because blocking sunlight is harmful.
B) No, because the owner is only using his property.
C) Only if the building exceeds a certain height.
D) Only if it was built without permission.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a property owner may build on his property even if it harms a neighbor, as long as he does not trespass or cause direct damage.

Water Rights and Property Modifications in Hanafi Fiqh
Four fatwas on property rights and water access, each with a specific answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four Hanafi rulings on water rights and property modifications: permissibility of raising a lower roof, building higher on a wall, blocking a river for irrigation, and preventing non-contributors from canal water.

Core Idea
Water rights and property modifications are permitted unless they harm others without justification, and communal works require contribution.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents four distinct fatwas addressing disputes over property modifications and water access. The first two rulings affirm that a property owner may alter his structure even if it affects a neighbor's drainage or view. The third ruling grants an upstream farmer priority to block a river for irrigation without downstream consent. The fourth ruling ties water rights in a collectively maintained canal to actual contribution, barring those who do not participate from benefiting.

Guided Path
1
Identify each fatwa and its subject
Read through the passage and label each question: (1) raising a lower roof, (2) building higher on a wall, (3) blocking a river for irrigation, (4) canal water access with khiraj and digging. Note that the first two deal with property modifications, the last two with water rights.
2
Analyze the rationale behind each permissive ruling
For the roof and wall cases, the principle is that one may use his property as he wishes (al-tasarruf fi al-milk) as long as he does not cause direct harm. For the river blocking case, the upstream farmer's need for irrigation outweighs the convenience of downstream users – a form of haqq al-irtifiq (prior right).
3
Examine the restriction in the canal case
The canal is dug using the khiraj fund and the labor of the villagers. Those who do not contribute (neither dig nor send someone) lose their right to water. This links water rights to contribution, a principle in Hanafi mu'amalat related to shirkat al-milk (joint ownership through contribution).
The word 'أمسحتهم' is likely 'مساحيهم' (their shovels).
4
Contrast the rulings with common misconceptions
Many assume water rights are equal for all upstream and downstream. These fatwas show that priority (sabaq) and contribution determine access, not mere proximity or payment of khiraj alone.
5
Apply the underlying principles to new cases
Consider a modern scenario: a public irrigation canal maintained by property taxes. If some landowners fail to pay the tax or contribute labor, can they be denied water? According to the fourth ruling, yes. The Hanafi principle of contribution-based access remains relevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسئل عن دارين لرجلين سطح أحدهما أعلى من الآخر ومسيل ماء العليا على الأخرى فأراد صاحب سطح السفلى أن يرفع سطحه أو يبني على سطحه علواً هل يحل له ذلك؟ قال : نعم، وفي التتمة سألت أبا حامد عن رجل له ضيعة أرضها مرتفعة هل يجوز له أن يسد النهر يوماً أو بعض يوم بغير رضا الأسافل حتى يسقيها ؟ قال : نعم . وسئل عن الرجل يبني على حائط نفسه بناء أزيد مما كان هل لجاره أن يمنعه؟ قال : لا وإن بلغ عنان السماء. وسئل أبو الفضل عمن يأخذ خراج القرية عن حفر النهر العظيم فيحفرونه بأمسحتهم من غير أن يصرف شيئاً من الخراج إلى الحفر وهناك من الأقوياء من لا يحفر ولا يبعث أحداً هل له أن يسقي منها أم لا؟ قال : يمنع من الماء .
He was asked about two houses belonging to two men, one roof higher than the other, and the water drainage from the higher to the lower: the owner of the lower roof wanted to raise his roof or build an upper story on it; is that permissible for him? He said: Yes. And in al-Tatimmah: I asked Abu Hamid about a man who has a farm whose land is elevated; is it permissible for him to block the river for a day or part of a day without the consent of those downstream in order to water it? He said: Yes. And he was asked about a man who raises a building on his own wall higher than before; does his neighbor have the right to prevent him? He said: No, even if it reaches the sky. And Abu al-Fadl was asked about a man who takes the khiraj of the village for digging the great canal, and they dig it with their shovels, without spending anything from the khiraj on digging, and there are strong people who neither dig nor send anyone; does he have the right to water from it or not? He said: He is prevented from water.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage compiles three distinct rulings on water rights. The first: a lower roof owner can raise his roof even if it affects the water flow from a higher roof; he is not obliged to keep the drainage path. The second: a farmer with elevated land may block a river for a day to irrigate his land without permission from downstream users, as he has priority to water his land. The third: regarding a collective canal dug using the khiraj (land tax) fund, if some powerful people do not contribute to the digging, they are not entitled to water; they are prevented from accessing the water. This last ruling emphasizes that water rights based on public works are linked to contribution. The first and second rulings highlight the primacy of one's own land and irrigation needs over others' convenience.

Key Terms
خراج (khiraj): Land tax imposed on agricultural land, here used to fund maintenance of canals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, a person who takes the khiraj but does not contribute to digging the canal:
A) Still has the right to water because he paid the tax.
B) Is prevented from water because he did not dig.
C) Has the right only if he is a landowner.
D) Can delegate the digging to someone else without consequence.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi water rights are not automatic; they depend on priority for natural sources and contribution for communal canals.

Selling Najis Substances: The Role of Custom
Ruling statement with custom-based reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling human excrement and animal dung and explain how custom (urf) determines permissibility.

Core Idea
Custom permits what logic forbids: animal dung sells freely, human waste only when mixed.

This passage opens a chapter on disliked sales. The first issue is the sale of impure but usable items. While both human excrement and animal dung are najis, the Hanafi school distinguishes them based on customary usage. Animal dung is widely used as fertilizer without hesitation, so its sale is permissible. Human excrement is not customarily used pure, so its sale is disliked unless mixed with ash or soil. This lesson explores how custom (urf) can transform a ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two substances and their basic rulings
The text states: 'karaha bay' al-'adhira' (he disliked the sale of human excrement) and 'la al-sarqin' (not animal dung). Note that the ruling for al-'adhira is makruh, while al-sarqin is permissible (mubah).
Al-'adhira = human excrement; al-sarqin = animal dung.
2
Understand the reasoning based on custom (urf)
The reason given: Muslims treat animal dung as wealth (tamawwul) and use it in all lands to increase crop yields. Human excrement is not customarily used in pure form; only when mixed with ash or soil does it become usable. Thus, the pure sale is disliked.
Custom (urf) is a recognized source of law in the Hanafi school.
3
Note the exception: mixing removes dislike
The text says: 'fa hinna'idh yajuzu bay'uha' – then its sale is permissible. When the impurity is mixed with ash or soil that predominates, the item becomes usable and the dislike vanishes.
This is an application of istihsan based on custom.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi difference
The text adds: 'al-sahih 'an al-imam anna al-intifa' bi al-'adhira al-khalisah ja'iz' – the correct view from Abu Hanifa is that benefiting from pure human excrement is permissible, thus selling it pure is permissible. However, the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq still records the dislike as the relied-upon view.
The difference is between the text's ruling (makruh) and Abu Hanifa's personal view (ja'iz). The mufta bihi is to avoid pure sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في البيع كره بيع العذرة لا السرقين لأن المسلمين يتمولون السرقين وانتفعوا به في سائر البلاد والأمصار من غير تكير فإنهم يلقونه في الأراضي لاستكثار الريع بخلاف العذرة لأن العادة لم تجر بالانتفاع بها إلا مخلوطة برماد أو تراب غالب عليها فحينئذ يجوز بيعها والصحيح عن الإمام أن الانتفاع بالعذرة الخالصة جائز بغلبة يجوز بيع الخالصة. وفي المحيط : رجل يبيع ويشتري على الطريق فأراد إنسان أن يشتري منه شيئاً، فإن لم يكن في قعوده ضرر بالناس وسعه أن يقعد في الطريق ويشتري منه، وإن كان فيه ضرر يكره له أن يشتري منه وهو المختار لأنه يكون معيناً له على الإثم والعدوان. صبي جاء إلى سوق بخبز أو بلبس أو بعدس فلا بأس بأن يبيع منه البصل والثوم وغير ذلك لأنه مأذون فيه عادة، ويكره أن منه الجوز والفستق حتى هل أذن له بذلك أبوه أم لا لأنه غير مأذون في ذلك عادة. وفيه : وأما المغني والنائحة والقوال. إذا أخذ المال هل يباح له إن كان من غير شرط يباح لأنه أعطاه المال عن طوع من غير عقد. وإن كان من عقد لا يباح له لأنه أجر على المعصية ا.هـ. وفي السراجية : يكره بيع الغلام الأمرد ممن عرف باللواطة رجل اشترى عبداً مجوسياً فأبى أن يسلم وقال إن بعتني من مسلم قتلت نفسي جاز له أن يبيعه من المجوسي، ولا بأس بأن الزنا من النصارى يبيع والقلنسوة من اليهود. وفي جامع الجوامع عن الثاني : باع ثوراً من المجوسي لينجروه في عيدهم يقتلوه بالعصا لا بأس به. وفي التتمة سئل علي بن أحمد أهل بلده زادوا في موازينهم فيما يوزن بزيادة فوق الزيادة في سائر البلدان وبعضهم يوافق وبعضهم لا يوافق أتحل لهم تلك الزيادة؟ فقال: لا. قالوا ولو أنفق الكل على ذلك؟ قال: لا . وفي السراجية : رجل اشترى لحماً أو سمكاً أو شيئاً من الثمار فذهب المشتري ليأتي بالثمن وأبطأ قخشي البائع أن
Chapter on Sales. He disliked the sale of human excrement, not animal dung, because Muslims consider animal dung as wealth and benefit from it in all lands and cities without concern; they put it on land to increase yield, unlike human excrement because custom has not permitted benefiting from it unless mixed with ash or soil that predominates over it; then its sale is permissible. The correct view from Imam Abu Hanifa is that benefiting from pure human excrement is permissible and thus selling the pure is permissible. In al-Muhit: A man sells and buys on the road; if someone wants to buy something from him, if his sitting causes no harm to people, it is permissible for him to sit on the road and buy from him; but if there is harm, it is disliked for him to buy from him, and this is the chosen view because he would be assisting him in sin and transgression. A child comes to the market with bread, or clothing, or lentils; there is no harm in buying onions, garlic, etc. from him because he is customarily permitted; but it is disliked to buy walnuts and pistachios from him whether or not his father has permitted him, because he is not customarily permitted in that. In it: As for the singer, wailing woman, and poet, if they take money, is it permissible for them? If it is without any condition, it is permissible because he gave the money voluntarily without a contract. If by contract, it is not permissible because it is wages for sin. End. In al-Sirajiyyah: It is disliked to sell a beardless boy to one known for sodomy. If a man bought a Zoroastrian slave and he refused to convert and said, 'If you sell me to a Muslim, I will kill myself,' it is permissible for him to sell him to a Zoroastrian. And there is no harm in the Zoroastrian selling and the hat of the Jews? In Jami' al-Jawami' from al-Thani: If one sells a bull to a Zoroastrian for them to slaughter it in their festival and kill it with sticks, there is no harm. In al-Tatimma: Ali ibn Ahmad was asked about a people in his town who increased their weights beyond the standard of other lands; some agree and some disagree; is that increase permissible? He said: No. They said: Even if all agree? He said: No. In al-Sirajiyyah: A man bought meat or fish or some fruit, then the buyer went to bring the price and delayed; the seller feared that...
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This section from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq opens a new chapter on disliked sales (fasl fi al-bay'). The first ruling: selling human excrement (al-'adhira) is disliked, whereas selling animal dung (al-sarqin) is not. The reasoning is custom (urf): Muslims treat animal dung as valuable property, using it widely for fertilizing fields without any misgivings. Human excrement, on the other hand, is not customarily used in its pure form; only when mixed with ash or soil does it become usable, and then its sale is permissible. The correct view from Abu Hanifa is that even pure human excrement may be used and sold. The commentary then branches into several ancillary rulings from various sources: selling on the road is disliked if it causes harm to passersby; buying from a child is permissible for items customarily sold by children (like onions, garlic) but disliked for luxury items (walnuts, pistachios) unless the father explicitly permits. Next, payment to singers, wailers, and poets is permissible if given voluntarily without a contract, but not if contracted, as it becomes wages for sin. Further, selling a beardless youth to a known sodomite is disliked. A Zoroastrian slave who refuses to convert and threatens suicide may be sold to a Zoroastrian. Selling a bull to a Zoroastrian for their festival slaughter is permissible. Finally, increasing weights beyond the standard is not allowed even if everyone agrees. These rulings demonstrate how Hanafi scholars apply principles of custom (urf), harm (darar), and facilitation of sin (i'ana ala al-ithm) to derive specific commercial guidelines.

Key Terms
العذرة (al-'adhira): Human excrement
السرقين (al-sarqin): Animal dung

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason why selling animal dung (al-sarqin) is not disliked according to the Hanafi school?
A) Animal dung is pure because it comes from halal animals.
B) Muslims customarily treat animal dung as valuable wealth and use it for fertilizing.
C) There is a specific hadith allowing the sale of dung.
D) It is not considered najis in the Hanafi school.

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) can transform the ruling on selling impure items: what is customarily used as wealth is permissible despite impurity.

Roadside Sales and Buying from Children
Two rulings on disliked sales based on harm and custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on buying from a roadside vendor and from a child, and apply the principles of harm (darar) and customary permission (idhn 'urfi).

Core Idea
Harm to others and lack of customary permission make a sale disliked.

This lesson covers two related scenarios: selling on a public road and buying from a child. The Hanafi school balances economic activity with preventing harm and respecting customary boundaries. If a vendor on the road causes no inconvenience, the transaction is fine; if it causes harm, it becomes disliked. Similarly, a child who comes to market with everyday items can be bought from, but luxury items like walnuts require explicit parental permission. This shows how urf and darar shape minor transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First scenario: a man sells goods on a public road. Second: a child comes to the market with basic goods (bread, clothing, lentils) and someone wants to buy from him.
These are distinct rulings linked by the theme of 'dislike due to potential harm or lack of permission'.
2
Analyze the roadside sale: harm as the criterion
If the vendor's sitting causes no harm to passersby, it is permissible (yasa'uhu). If it does cause harm, it is disliked (yukrahu) for the buyer to purchase from him, as he would be assisting in sin (i'ana 'ala al-ithm).
The chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that buying is disliked if there is harm.
3
Analyze buying from a child: customary permission
If the child sells items customarily sold by children (like onions, garlic), it is permissible because he is customarily permitted (ma'dhun fihi 'adatan). For items not customarily sold by children (like walnuts, pistachios), it is disliked regardless of explicit parental permission, as the child is not customarily authorized.
Custom (urf) determines the scope of the child's implied permission.
4
Understand the legal reasoning
For roadside sales, the principle is 'la darar wa la dirar'. For child vendors, the principle is that a child's transactions are valid only if customarily permitted; otherwise, they are suspended (mawquf) on guardian's consent, but buying without such consent is disliked.
The child's transaction is not void but makruh for the buyer.

Controversial Payments and Sales: Singers, Beardless Boys, and Zoroastrians
Multiple rulings on payment and sales involving potential sin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on payment to performers, sale to a known sodomite, and sale to Zoroastrians for religious purposes, and identify the principle of facilitating sin (i'ana 'ala al-ithm).

Core Idea
Payment without contract is permissible; with contract it is wages for sin. Selling to known sinners is disliked; selling for religious rituals is allowed if no direct sin.

This lesson covers three distinct but interconnected rulings: (1) payment to singers, wailers, and poets – permissible if voluntary, but haram if contracted; (2) selling a beardless youth to a known sodomite – disliked; (3) selling a bull to Zoroastrians for their festival – permissible. The thread is whether the transaction facilitates sin (i'ana 'ala al-ithm). The first two involve clear sin, while the third does not because killing an animal with sticks is not inherently sinful in itself (the Zoroastrian's belief does not affect the sale).

Guided Path
1
Analyze payment to singers, wailers, poets
If payment is given voluntarily without any prior contract (bi ghayr shart), it is permissible because the giver donates freely. If there is a contract for the performance, the payment becomes wages for sin (ujrat 'ala al-ma'siyah) and is not permissible for the performer to accept.
The sin here is that singing, wailing, and poetry may involve falsehood or mourning beyond permissible limits.
2
Understand the ruling on selling a beardless boy
It is disliked to sell a beardless youth (ghulam amrad) to a person known for sodomy (luwat) because it facilitates sin. This is based on the principle of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking the means to evil).
The dislike is for the seller; the transaction itself may be valid but sinful.
3
Examine the sale to Zoroastrians
A Zoroastrian slave who refuses to convert can be sold to another Zoroastrian if he threatens suicide. Also, selling a bull to Zoroastrians for them to kill in their festival with sticks is permissible (la ba'sa bihi). The reasoning: the act of killing with sticks is not inherently sinful from an Islamic perspective; it is their custom. The sale does not facilitate a prohibited act.
This shows the Hanafi school's emphasis on the objective nature of the act, not the subjective religious significance to the buyer.

Accepting a Single Report on Ownership
Ruling on accepting a report about contested ownership after sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a single report about contested ownership is accepted in Hanafi fiqh and apply them to different scenarios.

Core Idea
A single upright report is accepted only if no prior dispute existed; otherwise, it is rejected.

This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling on accepting a single person's report about ownership after a sale. The key distinction lies in whether there was a prior dispute between the buyer and seller. You will learn how apparent ownership is trusted and when an informer's report becomes inadmissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
The passage begins with a buyer who purchases a slave-girl, pays the price, and then the seller denies the sale. The buyer takes possession. The default ruling is that the buyer should not accept the seller's denial because he has apparent ownership.
Apparent ownership outweighs a mere denial.
2
Analyze the exception for no prior dispute
If the buyer did not witness the seller's denial (i.e., no prior altercation), he may accept the report of an upright informer that the item was usurped. If the informer is sinful, he uses his best judgment.
The informer's integrity matters only when there is no dispute.
3
Apply the condition of prior dispute
Muhammad states that once there has been altercation and denial from the original owner, the report of any informer (whether upright or sinful) is not accepted.
Past conflict taints the reliability of any subsequent report.
4
Recognize the underlying principle
The ruling is based on istihsan: practical need to trust apparent ownership and avoid fraud. Apparent ownership is relied upon until strong evidence (like a prior dispute) undermines it.
Istihsan overrides strict evidentiary rules in these cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكراهية. قال: اشتريت هذه الجارية من فلان ونقدته الثمن ثم جحد البائع البيع فأخذتها منه لا ينبغي له أن يقبل قوله. وفي فتاوى العتابية: ولو لم يذكر الجحود على الشراء منه ينبغي له أن يقبل قوله إذا كان عدلاً، وإن كان المخبر على الجحود فاسقاً فيه أكبر رأيه كما تقدم. وفي الفتاوى الغياثية: ولو ورثه أو أبيح له فأخبره عدل بأنه غصبه وكذبه ذو اليد فهو متهم فيجوز له أن يشتريها قال محمد: هذا إذا لم يجئ التشاجر والتجاحد من الذي كان يملك فإن جاءت المشاجرة والإنكار من المالك لا يقبل خبر المخبر، سواء كان فاسقاً أو عدلاً.
Book of Abominations. He said: I bought this slave-girl from so-and-so and paid him the price, then the seller denied the sale, so I took her from him—it is not proper for him to accept his statement. And in the Fatawa al-Atabiyyah: If he did not mention the denial upon purchasing from him, it is proper for him to accept his statement if he is upright, and if the informer of the denial is a sinner, he uses his best judgment as previously. And in the Fatawa al-Ghiyathiyyah: If he inherited or it was made permissible to him, and an upright person informed him that it was usurped and the possessor denied it, then he is suspect, so it is permissible for him to buy it. Muhammad said: This is when there has been no altercation and denial from the one who owned it. If there has been altercation and denial from the owner, the report of the informer is not accepted, whether he is a sinner or upright.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage, from the Book of Abominations, discusses the acceptance of a single report regarding ownership after a sale. The ruling is divided into scenarios. If a buyer purchases an item and the seller later denies the sale, the buyer should not accept the seller's statement (i.e., he may keep the item) because the buyer has apparent ownership through payment and possession. However, if the buyer did not witness the denial, he may accept an upright informer's report. In the case of inheritance or gift, if an upright person reports that the item was usurped, the new owner may buy it from the possessor only if there was no prior dispute. If the original owner had already disputed, the report is not accepted. The key principle is that apparent ownership is trusted until strong evidence contradicts it.

Key Terms
جحود (juhud): Denial of a claim, specifically here the seller denying the sale
تشاجر (tashājur): Quarrel or altercation between parties, indicating prior dispute
عدل (ʿadl): Upright, just; a person of reliable moral character

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it permissible to accept a single report that an item was usurped?
A) Always, if the informer is upright
B) Only if there has been no prior dispute between the parties
C) Only if the informer is a sinner
D) Never

Key Takeaway
A single report about ownership is accepted only when there is no prior dispute, even if the informer is upright; the presence of altercation renders the report inadmissible.

Five Cases Where a Single Report Is Not Accepted
Enumeration of five scenarios where a single witness's testimony is rejected

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five cases where a single person's testimony is not accepted in Hanafi fiqh and explain the underlying principle of protecting established rights.

Core Idea
A single report cannot overturn strong presumptions like ownership, marriage, or innocence.

In Islamic law, testimony is a powerful proof. However, the Hanafi school identifies five specific situations where the report of a single individual is legally disregarded. These exceptions protect established rights (e.g., ownership, marital status) from being overturned by unsubstantiated claims. In this lesson, we will examine each case and the rationale behind the rule.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the five categories
Use the mnemonic: Sale, Marriage, Food, Murder, (Fifth). Focus on the first four clear cases.
The fifth case is incomplete in the text; later scholars have filled it.
2
Identify the protected right
For each case, ask: which established right is being protected? Ownership in sale, marital bond in marriage, etc.
3
Understand the exception to acceptance
Note when a single report IS accepted (e.g., claim that food is haram) and why.
Religious caution (wara') overrides the general rule in some cases.
4
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Test your understanding by creating similar scenarios where a single report would be rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخزانة خمسة أشياء لا يقبل قول الواحد فيها: إذا اشترى شيئاً فأخبره رجل أنه لغير البائع وباعه بغير أمره لا يصدقه وجاز تصرفه فيه، وإذا تزوج فأخبره رجل أنها أخته من الرضاع ويتنزه عنها، وإذا اشترى طعاماً شراء فأخبره ثقة أنه حرام أو غصبه البائع لا يصدق في الغصب ويصدق في الحرام. رأي رجلاً قتل ولداً له بالسيف وجحد قتله لا يصدق ووسع من عاين ذلك أن قال محمد.
And in al-Khazanah: Five things in which the statement of a single person is not accepted: (1) If he bought something and a man informed him that it belongs to someone other than the seller and he sold it without his permission, he is not believed and his transaction is valid. (2) If he married and a man informed him that she is his foster-sister, he should abstain from her but the marriage is not invalidated. (3) If he bought food and a trustworthy person informed him that it is forbidden or that the seller usurped it, he is not believed regarding usurpation but he is believed regarding it being forbidden. (4) If a man saw someone kill his son with a sword and the killer denied it, he is not believed, but the eyewitness is at ease to act? Muhammad said...
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage from al-Khazanah lists five situations where the report of a single person is legally disregarded. First, in a sale, if a third party claims the item is not the seller's, the buyer is not obligated to believe him; the sale remains valid. Second, in marriage, if someone claims the wife is a foster relative, the husband should be cautious but the marriage is not automatically invalid. Third, in food purchases, a claim of usurpation is not accepted from a single informant, but a claim that the food is forbidden (haram) is accepted due to religious caution. Fourth, a witness to a killing whose report is denied is not believed in court. The fifth item is incomplete in the text but likely relates to similar scenarios. The underlying principle is that strong presumptions (like ownership or marriage validity) are not overturned by a single report.

Key Terms
يتنزه عنها (yatanazzahu 'anha): He should avoid her (the wife) out of caution, but the marriage remains valid
لا يصدقه (lā yusaddiqahu): He is not believed; the report is not legally accepted

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Claim that the seller usurped the item
B) Claim that food is forbidden (haram)
C) Claim that a wife is a foster sister
D) Claim that the item belongs to another

Key Takeaway
A single report is not accepted when it would overturn a strong presumption (ownership, marriage, innocence) except when religious caution (wara') demands accepting the report about haram.

Taking the Price of Wine Sold by a Muslim or Non-Muslim
Ruling on accepting wine price from Muslim vs. non-Muslim debtors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting the price of wine from a Muslim versus a non-Muslim debtor and explain the underlying distinction based on mal mutaqawwim.

Core Idea
Wine is valuable property for non-Muslims but not for Muslims, so its price may be taken from a non-Muslim seller but not from a Muslim.

When a debtor repays a loan using money obtained from selling wine, the permissibility for the creditor to accept that money depends on the seller's religion. This lesson explores the Hanafi position, the proof for the distinction, and how a judge's ruling affects the outward lawfulness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The creditor holds a debt; the debtor paid with the price of wine. First scenario: debtor is a Muslim who sold wine (ruling: impermissible). Second: debtor is a non-Muslim who sold wine (ruling: permissible).
The ruling is based on the seller's religion, not the buyer's.
2
Locate the legal basis
The proof is the concept of mal mutaqawwim. For a Muslim, wine is not valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim), so the sale is void (batil) and the seller does not own the price. For a non-Muslim, wine is valuable, so the sale is valid and the seller owns the price.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the effect of a judge's ruling
If a judge rules on the debt without knowing the money is from wine, it becomes lawful outwardly (zahiran) but not inwardly (batinan). This is because a judge's ruling can only affect outward appearances, as confirmed by Imam Muhammad.
Imam al-Zayla'i questioned this, but the relied-upon position affirms outward lawfulness.
4
Extend to other illicit earnings
The same principle applies to earnings from singing, nabidh, oppression, or bribery: they are like usurped property (majhul al-malik) and must be returned to owners if known, or given to charity.
This extension is based on qiyas and caution (ihtiyat).
5
Resolve cross-religion sales
If a Muslim sells wine to a non-Muslim, or a non-Muslim to a Muslim, the correct view is to give preference to prohibition (tarjih al-muharram), i.e., consider the Muslim seller's perspective to declare the price unlawful.
This cautious approach is adopted to avoid facilitating sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره لرب الدين أخذ ثمن خمر باعها مسلم لا كافر يعني إذا كان لشخص مسلم دين على مسلم فباع الذي عليه الدين خمراً وأخذ ثمنها وقضى الدين لا يحل للمدين أن يأخذ ذلك بدينه، وإن كان البائع كافراً جاز له أن يأخذ والفرق أن البيع في الوجه الأول باطل فلم يملك البائع الثمن وهو باق على ملك المشتري فلا يحل له أن يأخذ مال الغير بغير رضاه، والبيع في الوجه الثاني صحيح فملك البائع الثمن لأن الخمر مال متقوم في حق الكافر فجاز له الأخذ بخلاف المسلم. وفي النهاية عن محمد هذا إذا كان القضاء والاقتضاء بالرضا، فإن كان بقضاء القاضي فقضى عليه بهذا الثمن ولم يعلم القاضي بكونه ثمن خمر يطيب له ذلك بقضائه واستشكل الإمام الزيلعي حيث قال : إنه مال الغير فكيف يطيب له بقضاء القاضي ومحمد لا يرى نفوذ قضاء القاضي باطناً وإنما ينفذ عنده ظاهراً. ولو مات مسلم وترك ثمن خمر باعها لا يحل لورثته أن يأخذوا ذلك لأنه كالمغصوب . قال في النهاية قال بعض مشايخنا : كسب المغنية كالمغصوب لم يحل لأحد أخذه قالوا وعلى هذا لو مات رجل وكسبه من ثمن الباذق والظلم أو أخذ الرشوة تعود الورثة ولا يأخذون منه شيئاً وهو الأولى لهم ويردونه على أربابه إن عرفوهم وإلا يتصدقوا به لأن سبيل الكسب الخبيث التصدق إذا تعذر الرد. وظاهر هذا أن المعتبر اعتقاد البائع سواء باعه من مسلم أو كافر، فإن كان البائع مسلماً لا يملك ذلك الثمن. اشتراه منه مسلم أو كافر. وإن كان كافراً ملك الثمن سواء اشتراه منه مسلم أو کافر ا هـ . فإن قيل : هذا ظاهر إذا باع الخمر المسلم للمسلم أو الكافر للكافر، وأما إذا باع المسلم للكافر أو الكافر للمسلم فلم لم يقبل اعتقاد الكافر فنقول بالجواز أو باعتقاد المسلم فنقول بعدم الجواز؟ قلنا : الأصح ترجيح المحرم.
And it is disliked for the creditor to take the price of wine sold by a Muslim, not by a non-Muslim. Meaning: If a Muslim person has a debt upon a Muslim, and the debtor sells wine and takes its price and pays the debt, it is not permissible for the creditor to take that as his debt. But if the seller was a non-Muslim, it is permissible for him to take it. The difference is that the sale in the first case is void, so the seller did not own the price, and it remains the property of the buyer, so it is not permissible for him to take someone else's property without his consent. The sale in the second case is valid, so the seller owned the price because wine is considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) with respect to the non-Muslim, so it is permissible for him to take it, unlike the Muslim. In al-Nihaya from Muhammad: This is when the payment and collection are by mutual consent. If it was by a judge's ruling, and he ruled for this price without knowing it was the price of wine, it becomes lawful for him by virtue of the ruling. Imam al-Zayla'i objected, saying: It is someone else's property, how can it become lawful by a judge's ruling when Muhammad does not consider the judge's ruling effective inwardly, but only outwardly. And if a Muslim dies leaving the price of wine he sold, it is not permissible for his heirs to take it because it is like a usurped property. In al-Nihaya, some of our teachers said: The earnings of a singing girl are like usurped property, not permissible for anyone to take. They said: Similarly, if a man dies and his earnings are from the price of nabidh (date wine), oppression, or bribery, the heirs should not take anything from it, and it is better for them to return it to its owners if known, otherwise give it in charity, because the way of evil earnings is charity when return is impossible. The apparent meaning is that consideration is given to the belief of the seller, whether he sold to a Muslim or non-Muslim. If the seller is Muslim, he does not own that price, whether bought from him by a Muslim or non-Muslim. If he is a non-Muslim, he owns the price, whether bought from him by a Muslim or non-Muslim. If it is said: This is clear when the Muslim sells to a Muslim or the non-Muslim to a non-Muslim. But if a Muslim sells to a non-Muslim or a non-Muslim to a Muslim, why not consider the belief of the non-Muslim and say it is permissible? Or the belief of the Muslim and say it is not permissible? We say: The correct view is that the prohibition is given preference.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on taking the price of wine in the context of debt repayment. The general principle: if a Muslim sells wine, the sale is void because wine is not valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) for Muslims. Therefore, the seller does not own the price, and it remains the property of the buyer. Consequently, a creditor cannot accept such money as payment because it is not the debtor's lawful property. However, if a non-Muslim sells wine, the sale is valid for him (as wine is valuable for him), so he owns the price, and a creditor may accept it. The text then discusses nuances: if a judge rules on such a transaction without knowledge, it is lawful outwardly, but inwardly the money remains unlawful. The ruling extends to other illicit earnings like bribery, which should be returned or given to charity. The final discussion considers cross-religion sales: the prevailing view is to prioritize the prohibition (i.e., consider the Muslim seller's perspective to declare the price unlawful), as the school takes a cautious approach regarding wine.

Key Terms
ثمن الخمر (thaman al-khamr): The price received from selling wine; its ruling depends on the seller's religion.
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property recognized as valuable in Islamic law; wine is not such for Muslims but is for non-Muslims.
قضاء القاضي (qada' al-qadi): A judge's ruling; it can make a transaction lawful outwardly even if not inwardly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the non-Muslim owner of the wine owns its price.
B) No, because wine is always impure.
C) Yes, but only if the creditor is also a non-Muslim.
D) No, because the sale is void.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes the permissibility of taking the price of wine based on the seller's religion: permissible from a non-Muslim, impermissible from a Muslim.

Taking the Price of Wine as Debt Repayment: Ruling and Reasoning
Commentary on the ruling of taking wine price to repay debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a creditor accepting wine proceeds from a Muslim debtor and explain the legal reasoning based on the concept of mal mutaqawwim.

Core Idea
Whether a creditor may accept wine proceeds depends on the seller's religion: a Muslim cannot own wine's price, so the transaction is void.

In Hanafi fiqh, the permissibility of accepting money from a transaction hinges on the validity of the underlying sale. This lesson examines the case where a debtor uses the proceeds from selling wine to repay a debt. You will see how the concept of 'mal mutaqawwim' (legally valuable property) determines the ruling, and why the seller's religion is decisive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The creditor's act of taking the price of wine as debt repayment. The ruling: makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) if the seller is Muslim; permissible if the seller is non-Muslim.
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is the Hanafi principle that wine is not mal mutaqawwim for Muslims, making the sale invalid, so the seller does not own the price.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Since the sale is invalid, the price remains the buyer's property; the creditor would be taking another's property without consent, which is prohibited.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is an intra-Hanafi nuance: if a judge rules for the debt without knowing the source, Muhammad al-Shaybani holds it becomes permissible outwardly, but Imam al-Zayla'i objects because the seller still does not own the price in reality.
5
Evaluate the extension
The same principle applies to other unlawful earnings (bribes, oppression, etc.): heirs cannot inherit them; they must be returned or given in charity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وكره لرب الدين أخذ ثمن خمر باعها مسلم لا كافر يعني إذا كان لشخص مسلم دين على مسلم فباع الذي عليه الدين خمراً وأخذ ثمنها وقضى الدين لا يحل للمدين أن يأخذ ذلك بدينه، وإن كان البائع كافراً جاز له أن يأخذ والفرق أن البيع في الوجه الأول باطل فلم يملك البائع الثمن وهو باق على ملك المشتري فلا يحل له أن يأخذ مال الغير بغير رضاه، والبيع في الوجه الثاني صحيح فملك البائع الثمن لأن الخمر مال متقوم في حق الكافر فجاز له الأخذ بخلاف المسلم. وفي النهاية عن محمد هذا إذا كان القضاء والاقتضاء بالرضا، فإن كان بقضاء القاضي فقضى عليه بهذا الثمن ولم يعلم القاضي بكونه ثمن خمر يطيب له ذلك بقضائه واستشكل الإمام الزيلعي حيث قال : إنه مال الغير فكيف يطيب له بقضاء القاضي ومحمد لا يرى نفوذ قضاء القاضي باطناً وإنما ينفذ عنده ظاهراً. ولو مات مسلم وترك ثمن خمر باعها لا يحل لورثته أن يأخذوا ذلك لأنه كالمغصوب . قال في النهاية قال بعض مشايخنا : كسب المغنية كالمغصوب لم يحل لأحد أخذه قالوا وعلى هذا لو مات رجل وكسبه من ثمن الباذق والظلم أو أخذ الرشوة تعود الورثة ولا يأخذون منه شيئاً وهو الأولى لهم ويردونه على أربابه إن عرفوهم وإلا يتصدقوا به لأن سبيل الكسب الخبيث التصدق إذا تعذر الرد. وظاهر هذا أن المعتبر اعتقاد البائع سواء باعه من مسلم أو كافر، فإن كان البائع مسلماً لا يملك ذلك الثمن. اشتراه منه مسلم أو كافر. وإن كان كافراً ملك الثمن سواء اشتراه منه مسلم أو کافر ا هـ . فإن قيل : هذا ظاهر إذا باع الخمر المسلم للمسلم أو الكافر للكافر، وأما إذا باع المسلم للكافر أو الكافر للمسلم فلم لم يقبل اعتقاد الكافر فنقول بالجواز أو باعتقاد المسلم فنقول بعدم الجواز؟ قلنا : الأصح ترجيح المحرم.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (It is disliked for the owner of a debt to take the price of wine sold by a Muslim, not a non-Muslim). Meaning: If a Muslim has a debt on a Muslim, and the debtor sold wine and took its price and repaid the debt, it is not permissible for the creditor to take that as his debt. But if the seller is a non-Muslim, it is permissible for him to take. The difference is that the sale in the first case is invalid, so the seller did not own the price, and it remains the property of the buyer, so it is not permissible for him to take another's property without his consent. In the second case, the sale is valid, so the seller owned the price because wine is considered valuable property for the non-Muslim, so it is permissible for him to take, unlike the Muslim. And in al-Nihaya from Muhammad: This is if the repayment and taking are by mutual consent. But if it is by the judge's ruling and he ruled for this price without knowing it is the price of wine, it becomes permissible for him by his ruling. Imam al-Zayla'i objected, saying: It is another's property, so how can it become permissible by the judge's ruling? And Muhammad does not consider the judge's ruling effective inwardly, only outwardly. And if a Muslim died and left the price of wine he sold, it is not permissible for his heirs to take it because it is like usurped property. He said in al-Nihaya: Some of our scholars said: The earnings of a singing girl are like usurped property; it is not permissible for anyone to take it. They said: Likewise, if a man died and his earnings came from the price of badhiq (a type of wine) and oppression or taking bribes, the heirs [should] return it and not take anything from it, and it is better for them to return it to its owners if they know them, otherwise they should give it in charity, because the way of evil earnings is charity when return is impossible. The apparent meaning is that the consideration is the belief of the seller, whether he sold to a Muslim or non-Muslim. If the seller is Muslim, he does not own that price, regardless of whether the buyer is Muslim or non-Muslim. And if the seller is non-Muslim, he owns the price, regardless of whether the buyer is Muslim or non-Muslim. Then it is said: This is apparent when the Muslim sells to Muslim or non-Muslim to non-Muslim. But if a Muslim sells to a non-Muslim or a non-Muslim sells to a Muslim, why is the non-Muslim's belief not accepted so we say it is permissible, or the Muslim's belief so we say it is not permissible? We say: The correct view is to give precedence to the prohibition.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn ruling that it is makruh (disliked) for a creditor to take the price of wine sold by a Muslim debtor, but not if the debtor is a non-Muslim. The commentator, Ibn Nujaym, explains the reasoning: When a Muslim sells wine, the sale is invalid (batil) in Hanafi law because wine is not considered property (mal mutaqawwim) for Muslims. Therefore, the seller does not own the price; it remains the property of the buyer. So the creditor cannot take it as debt repayment because it is someone else's property. For a non-Muslim seller, the sale is valid because wine is considered valuable property for them (since they are not bound by Islamic prohibition), so the seller owns the price and can repay the debt from it. The commentary then discusses a nuance: if the debt was settled by a judge's ruling without knowing the source, some say it becomes permissible outwardly (according to Muhammad al-Shaybani). Imam al-Zayla'i objected, but the commentator notes Muhammad's view that the judge's ruling is effective only externally, not in the inner reality. The passage then extends the ruling to the case of a deceased Muslim leaving wine proceeds: the heirs cannot take it because it is like usurped property. Similarly, earnings from singing, badhiq (intoxicating drink), oppression, and bribery are unlawful to inherit; the heirs should return them to the original owners or give in charity. The key principle is that the seller's religion determines ownership: a Muslim cannot own the price of wine, so any transaction involving it is invalid, while a non-Muslim can.

Key Terms
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable and inviolable; wine is not mutaqawwim for Muslims but is for non-Muslims in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They may inherit it as part of the estate.
B) They must return it to the buyer if known, or give it in charity.
C) They should keep it for themselves.
D) They should destroy it.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of accepting wine proceeds as debt repayment hinges on the seller's religion because a Muslim cannot own non-mutaqawwim property (wine).

Ruling on Hoarding (Ihtikar) in Hanafi Fiqh
Commentary on the ruling of hoarding food and other necessities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hoarding, identify the evidence from hadith, and distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on the scope of ihtikar.

Core Idea
Hoarding is prohibited when it harms the public, but the definition of what counts as hoarding differs among Hanafi imams.

This lesson examines the ruling on ihtikar (hoarding) as presented in al-Bahr al-Raiq. The text establishes that hoarding is disliked (makruh tahrimi) due to the hadith cursing the hoarder and the principle of public welfare. However, the Hanafi school has an internal disagreement over what types of goods are covered and whether hoarding applies when goods are brought from outside the town.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is ihtikar? What is the hukm level according to the text?
The text uses 'يكره' which in this context indicates makruh tahrimi.
2
Locate the dalil
Which hadith is cited, and what is the wajh al-dalalah? The hadith curses the hoarder, indicating prohibition.
Dalil is a hadith, ahad, zanni al-thubut and dalala.
3
Analyze the condition
When does hoarding become disliked? Only in a small town where it harms the people. In a large city it is not disliked.
Condition: harm to the public in a small town.
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
How do Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad differ on the scope of hoarding? Abu Hanifa restricts to food; Abu Yusuf extends to anything causing harm; Muhammad extends to the outskirts of the town.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: hoarding clothes or money is disliked according to Abu Yusuf but not according to Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (واحتكار قوت الآدميين والبهائم في بلد لم يضر بأهلها) يعني يكره الاحتكار في بلد يضر بأهلها لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام الجالب» مرزوق والمحتكر ملعون»(۱) ولأنه تعلق به حق العامة وفي الامتناع عن البيع ابطال حقهم وتضييق الأمر عليهم فيكره . هذا إذا كانت البلدة صغيرة يضر ذلك بأهلها، أما إذا كانت كبيرة فلا يكره لأنه حابس ملكه. وتخصيص الاحتكار بالأقوات قول الإمام والثالث وقال أبو يوسف: كل ما يضر العامة فهو احتكار بالأقوات كان أو ثياباً أو دارهم أو دنانير اعتباراً لحقيقة الضرر لأنه هو المؤثر في الكراهة وهما اعتبر الحبس المتعارف وهو الحاصل في الأقوات في المدة فإذا قصرت لا يكون احتكاراً لعدم الضرر إذا طالت يكون مكروهاً. ثم قيل : هو مقدر بأربعين ليلة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام من احتكر طعاماً أربعين ليلة فهو بريء من وقيل بالشهر لأن ما دونه قليل عاجل وهو ما فوقه كثير آجل، ويقع التفاوت في المأثم بين أن يتربص العسرة وبين أن يتربص القحط والعياذ بالله . وقيل المدة المذكورة للمعاقبة في الدنيا وأما الأثم فيحصل وإن قلت المدة. فحاصله أن التجارة في الطعام غير محمودة. وفي المحيط : الاحتكار على وجوه : أحدها أن يشتري في المصر عند الحاجة إليه، ولو اشترى طعاماً . في غير المصر ونقله إلى المصر وحبسه قال الإمام : لا بأس به لأن حق العامة إنما يتعلق بما جمع من المصر أو جلب من فنائه . وقال الثاني : يكره وقال محمد : كل بقعة يمتد منها إلى المصر في العادة فهي بمنزلة فناء المصر يحرم الاحتكار منه وهذا في غاية الاحتياط ا هـ .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the hoarding of food for humans and animals in a town that does not harm its people) means: It is disliked to hoard in a town that harms its people, because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'The one who brings goods is blessed, and the hoarder is cursed.' And because the public right is attached to it, and refraining from selling invalidates their right and causes them difficulty, so it is disliked. This is if the town is small and that harms its people. As for if it is large, it is not disliked because he is withholding his own property. And the specification of hoarding to food is the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa and the third [imam]. Abu Yusuf said: Everything that harms the public is hoarding, whether food, clothes, dirhams or dinars, considering the reality of harm because it is what affects the dislike. And the two considered the customary withholding which occurs in food over a period. If it is short, it is not hoarding due to lack of harm; if it is long, it becomes disliked. Then it was said: It is defined by forty nights, because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever hoards food for forty nights, he is free from...' And it was said: a month, because what is less than that is little and immediate, and what is above it is much and deferred. And the sin differs between waiting for hardship and waiting for famine, may Allah protect us. And it was said the mentioned period is for punishment in this world, and as for sin, it occurs even if the period is short. The summary is that trade in food is not praiseworthy. In al-Muhit: Hoarding is of types: one is to buy in the town when there is need for it. If one buys food outside the town and transports it to the town and withholds it, Imam Abu Hanifa said: There is no harm because the public right attaches only to what is gathered in the town or brought from its outskirts. And the second [Abu Yusuf] said: It is disliked. And Muhammad said: Every area that is normally considered as the outskirts of the town is like the town's periphery; hoarding from it is prohibited. This is extreme caution.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling about hoarding (ihtikar). The matn states that hoarding is disliked when it harms the people. The commentator explains that the hadith 'the hoarder is cursed' supports this. Hoarding is disliked in a small town where it causes harm; in a large city, it is not disliked because the supply is sufficient. There is an intra-madhab dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa restricts hoarding to food staples, while Abu Yusuf includes anything that harms the public, such as clothing or currency. The duration of hoarding also matters: some say forty nights, some say a month, but the sin may occur even with a short wait. The text further discusses types of hoarding: buying in the town when needed is hoarding; bringing from outside and withholding is not hoarding according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad have stricter views. The cautious position is to avoid any hoarding that resembles it.

Key Terms
احتكار (ihtikar): Hoarding goods, especially food, to sell at a high price, causing harm to the public.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when is hoarding not disliked?
A) When the hoarded goods are only clothes.
B) When the town is large and the public is not harmed.
C) When the hoarding lasts less than 40 days.
D) When the goods are brought from outside the town.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on hoarding balances private property rights with public welfare, and the definition of hoarding must consider actual harm.
